The Book of Patience and Gratitude  
Which is the Second Book of the Quarter of Deliverances from The Revival of the Religious Sciences  

In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.  

Praise be to God, the Worthy of praise and commendation, the Unique in the mantle of majesty, the One in attributes of glory and exaltation, Who supports the chosen saints with the strength of patience in ease and hardship, and gratitude in trial and blessing. And blessings upon Muhammad, master of the prophets, and upon his companions, leaders of the pure, and upon his family, guides of the righteous and pious—a blessing preserved perpetually from extinction and safeguarded in succession from interruption and cessation.  

Now then: Faith is two halves: one half patience and one half gratitude, as transmitted in the traditions and attested by the reports. They are also two attributes from the attributes of God Most High and two names from His beautiful names, for He named Himself the Patient and the Thankful. Thus, ignorance of the reality of patience and gratitude is ignorance of both halves of faith, and it is heedlessness of two attributes of the Merciful. There is no way to attain nearness to God Most High except through faith, and how can traversing the path of faith be conceived without knowing that by which faith exists and He by whom faith exists? Neglecting the knowledge of patience and gratitude is neglecting the knowledge of He by whom faith exists and the realization of that by which faith exists. How great is the need for both halves to be clarified and explained! We shall clarify both halves in one book due to their interconnection, God willing.  

The first half concerns patience, and within it is an exposition of the virtue of patience, an explanation of its definition and reality, an explanation of it being half of faith, an explanation of the variation of its names according to its referents, an explanation of its categories based on differences in strength and weakness, an explanation of the occasions requiring patience, and an explanation of the remedy of patience and what aids in it. These are seven chapters encompassing all its purposes, God willing.  

(Exposition of the Virtue of Patience)  

God Most High has described the patient with attributes and mentioned patience in the Qur’an in over seventy places. He ascribed most degrees and blessings to patience and made them its fruit, saying, exalted is He: “And We made from among them leaders guiding by Our command when they were patient” (32:24). And He said: “And the best word of your Lord was fulfilled for the Children of Israel because they were patient” (7:137). And He said: “And We will surely give those who were patient their reward according to the best of what they used to do” (16:96). And He said: “Those will be given their reward twice for what they patiently endured” (28:54). And He said: “Indeed, the patient will be given their reward without account” (39:10). Thus, there is no act of devotion except that its reward is by measure and reckoning—except patience. Due to fasting being from patience and that it is half of patience, God said: “Fasting is for Me, and I will reward it,” attributing it to Himself among all other acts of worship. And He promised the patient that He is with them, saying: “And be patient, indeed God is with the patient” (2:153). And He made victory contingent upon patience, saying: “Yes, if you remain patient and conscious of God and they come upon you suddenly, your Lord will reinforce you with five thousand angels having marks” (3:125). And He combined for the patient matters not combined for others, saying: “Those are the ones upon whom are blessings from their Lord and mercy, and it is they who are the guided” (2:157). Thus, guidance, mercy, and blessings are combined for the patient. Exhausting all verses regarding the station of patience would be lengthy.  

As for the reports, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: “Patience is half of faith,” as will come the explanation of it being half. And he said: “Among the least you have been given is certainty and resolve of patience. Whoever is given his share of these two, he will not care what he missed of night prayer and daytime fasting. And that you remain patient upon what you are upon is more beloved to me than that each of you meets me with the like of the deeds of all of you. But I fear that the world will open up to you after me, and then some of you will deny others, and the people of heaven will deny you. At that time, whoever is patient and expects reward will attain the perfection of his reward.” Then he recited the saying of God Most High: “Whatever you have will end, but what God has is lasting. And We will surely give those who were patient their reward” (16:96).  

Jabir reported that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about faith, and he said: “Patience and magnanimity.” He also said: “Patience is a treasure from the treasures of Paradise.” And he was once asked, “What is faith?” He said: “Patience.” This resembles his saying: “The Hajj is ‘Arafah,” meaning the essence of Hajj is ‘Arafah. He also said: “The best of deeds are those that the souls are averse to.”  

It is said that God Most High revealed to David (peace be upon him): “Adorn yourself with My attributes, and among My attributes is that I am the Patient.” In a narration from ‘Ata’ from Ibn ‘Abbas, when the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) visited the Ansar, he said: “Are you believers?” They remained silent. ‘Umar said: “Yes, O Messenger of God.” He said: “What is the sign of your faith?” They said: “We are grateful in prosperity, patient in adversity, and content with decree.” He said: “Believers, by the Lord of the Ka‘bah.”

(Kaaba)[9] And He, exalted be He, said: "In patience over what you dislike is much good."[10] And Jesus, peace be upon him, said: "You will not attain what you love except through your patience with what you dislike." And the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said: "If patience were a man, he would be noble, and God loves the patient."[11] The reports on this are innumerable.

As for the narrations, it is found in the letter of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, may God be pleased with him, to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari: "Hold fast to patience, and know that patience is of two kinds, one better than the other: Patience in calamity is good, but better than it is patience in abstaining from what God, exalted be He, has forbidden. And know that patience is the mainstay of faith, for piety is the best of righteousness, and piety is attained through patience." And 'Ali, may God honor his countenance, said: "Faith is built upon four pillars: certainty, patience, striving, and justice." He also said: "Patience in relation to faith is like the head to the body. There is no body for one who has no head, and no faith for one who has no patience." And 'Umar, may God be pleased with him, used to say: "How excellent are the two just dues, and how excellent is the bonus for the patient," meaning by the two just dues: prayer and mercy, and by the bonus: guidance. The 'bonus' is what is carried atop the two just dues on a camel, alluding to the saying of God, exalted be He: "Those are the ones upon whom are blessings from their Lord and mercy, and it is they who are the [rightly] guided." {2:157} And Habib ibn Abi Habib, when he recited this verse: "Indeed, We found him patient, an excellent servant. Indeed, he was one repeatedly turning back [to Allah]." {38:44} would weep and say: "How wondrous! He gave and He praised," meaning He is the Giver of patience and He is the Praiser. And Abu al-Darda' said: "The pinnacle of faith is patience with the decree and contentment with destiny." This is the exposition of the virtue of patience from the standpoint of transmitted texts.

As for from the standpoint of intellectual consideration, you will not understand it except after understanding the reality and meaning of patience, for knowing the virtue and rank is knowing an attribute, and it is not attained before knowing the described entity. Therefore, let us mention its reality and meaning, and with God is success.

(Exposition of the Reality and Meaning of Patience)

Know that patience is a station among the stations of religion and a stage among the stages of the wayfarers. All stations of religion are only organized from three matters: cognitions, states, and works. The cognitions are the foundations, and they beget the states, and the states yield the works. Thus, the cognitions are like trees, the states like branches, and the works like fruits. This pattern holds for all the stages of the wayfarers to God, exalted be He. The name 'faith' is sometimes specific to the cognitions and sometimes applied to the whole, as we mentioned regarding the difference between the names 'faith' and 'Islam' in the book "The Foundations of Beliefs." Likewise, patience is not complete except with a preceding cognition and an established state, for patience, in truth, is an expression of them, and the work is like the fruit that issues from them. This is not known except by knowing the manner of arrangement between the angels, humans, and beasts, for patience is a particular characteristic of humans and is not conceivable in beasts or angels. As for beasts, it is due to their deficiency. As for angels, it is due to their perfection. The explanation is that beasts are subjected to appetites and become subservient to them. They have no motivator for movement or stillness except appetite, and there is no power within them that opposes the appetite and restrains it from its demand, such that the steadfastness of that power in confronting the demand of the appetite could be called patience.

As for the angels, peace be upon them, they are purified for yearning towards the presence of Lordship and rejoicing in the degree of proximity to it. Appetite that diverts or hinders from it has not been given authority over them, such that they would need to combat what diverts them from the presence of Majesty with another force that overcomes the diverters. As for the human, he is created at the beginning of childhood deficient, like a beast. Nothing is created in him except the appetite for nourishment, which he needs. Then appears in him the appetite for play and adornment, then the appetite for marriage, in sequence. He has no power of patience whatsoever, for patience is an expression of the steadfastness of one force in opposition to another force, between which combat has arisen due to the contradiction of their demands and requirements. In the child, there is only the force of caprice, as in beasts. However, God, exalted be He, by His grace and vast generosity, honored the children of Adam and elevated their rank above the rank of beasts. He assigned to him, upon the completion of his personhood near puberty, two angels: one guides him, and the other strengthens him. Thus, he became distinguished, with the help of the two angels, from the beasts, and was endowed with two characteristics: One is the knowledge of God, exalted be He, knowledge of His Messenger, and knowledge of the benefits pertaining to ultimate outcomes—all of this attained from the angel to whom guidance and instruction belong. The beast has no knowledge and no guidance towards the benefit of ultimate outcomes, but only towards the demand of its appetites in the immediate moment. Therefore, it seeks only what is pleasurable. As for beneficial medicine, even if it is harmful in the immediate moment, it does not seek it nor know it. Thus, the human, by the light of guidance, knows that following appetites has disliked consequences in the ultimate outcome. But this guidance was not sufficient as long as there was not...

He possesses the capacity to abandon what is harmful, yet how many harmful things a person recognizes—such as an affliction befalling him, for example—but has no power to repel it? Thus he becomes in need of a power and strength by which to strike at the throat of desires, striving against them with that strength until he severs their enmity from himself. Therefore, God Almighty has assigned to him another angel who guides him, supports him, and strengthens him with troops you have not seen, and He has commanded this host to fight the host of desire. At times this host weakens, and at times that one strengthens, according to how God Almighty supplies His servant with divine support. Just as the light of guidance also varies among creation in a boundless diversity, let us name this quality by which man differs from beasts in suppressing and subduing desires the "religious impulse," and let us name the demands of desires according to their exigencies the "impulse of passion." Understand that a battle is ongoing between the religious impulse and the impulse of passion, and the war between them is back-and-forth; the battlefield of this combat is the heart of the servant. The reinforcements for the religious impulse come from the angels who aid the party of God Almighty, while the reinforcements for the impulse of desire come from the devils who aid the enemies of God Almighty. Patience, then, is the steadfastness of the religious impulse in confronting the impulse of desire. If it remains firm until it overcomes it and persists in opposing desire, then the servant has aided the party of God and joined the patient ones. But if he falters and weakens until desire overcomes him and he does not persevere in repelling it, he joins the followers of devils. For abandoning desired actions is a deed that yields a state called patience, which is the steadfastness of the religious impulse in opposition to the impulse of desire. And the steadfastness of the religious impulse is a state produced by knowledge of the enmity of desires and their opposition to the causes of happiness in this world and the next. When his certainty—meaning the knowledge called faith, which is conviction that desire is an enemy cutting off the path to God Almighty—strengthens, then the steadfastness of the religious impulse strengthens. And when its steadfastness strengthens, actions are completed contrary to what desire demands. Thus, abandoning desire is only achieved through the strength of the religious impulse opposing the impulse of desire, and through the strength of knowledge and faith that make the consequences of desires ugly and their end evil. These two angels are the ones entrusted with these two hosts by the permission and subjugation of God Almighty, and they are among the noble recorders—the two angels assigned to every human being. And when you know that the rank of the guiding angel is higher than that of the strengthening angel, it becomes clear to you that the right side is the nobler of the two sides of the ledger, which should be submitted to; thus he is the companion of the right, and the other is the companion of the left. The servant has two phases: heedlessness and reflection, indulgence and struggle. In heedlessness, he turns away from the companion of the right and wrongs him, so his offenses are recorded as evil deeds. In reflection, he turns to him to obtain guidance, thus doing good to him, so his turning is recorded as a good deed. Likewise, in indulgence, he turns away from the companion of the left, abandoning seeking aid from him, thus wronging him, and an evil deed is recorded against him. In struggle, he seeks aid from his troops, so a good deed is recorded for him. These good and evil deeds are recorded by their inscription, hence they are called noble recorders—"noble" because the servant benefits from their nobility and because all angels are noble and righteous, and "recorders" because they record the good and evil deeds. They write on scrolls folded within the secret of the heart and hidden from the secret of the heart, so that none may perceive them in this world, for they, their writing, their script, their attributes, and all that pertains to them belong to the realm of the unseen and the dominion, not to the visible world. Everything from the realm of dominion is not beheld by eyes in this world. Then these scrolls folded away from him are unfolded twice: once at the minor resurrection and once at the major resurrection. By the minor resurrection, I mean the state of death, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: "Whoever dies, his resurrection has come." In this resurrection, the servant is alone, and then it is said: "You have come to Us alone, as We created you the first time," and it is said: "Sufficient is yourself this day as a reckoner against you." As for the major resurrection, which gathers all creation, he is not alone but may be called to account before an assembly of creatures, and therein the righteous are led to Paradise and the criminals to the Fire in groups, not individually. The first terror is the terror of the minor resurrection, and all the terrors of the major resurrection have a counterpart in the minor resurrection—such as the quaking of the earth, for example. Your own personal earth quakes in death, for you know that if a quake befalls a town, it is truthfully said that their earth has quaked, even if the surrounding lands have not quaked. Indeed, if only a person's dwelling quakes, the quaking has occurred in his regard, because he is only harmed by the quaking of his own dwelling, not by the quaking of another's dwelling. Thus is his portion.

From the quaking, it has been fully realized without diminishment. Know that you are the most content creature formed from dust, and your exclusive portion from the dust is solely your body. As for the body of another, it is not your portion. The earth upon which you sit is, in relation to that, merely a setting and a place. You only fear its quaking because your body might quake on account of it; otherwise, the air is perpetually quivering, yet you do not dread it, since your body does not quake from it. Thus, your portion from the quaking of the entire earth is only the quaking of your body. It is your earth and your private dust. Your bones are the mountains of your earth, your head is the sky of your earth, your heart is the sun of your earth, your hearing and sight and all your faculties are the stars of your sky, the sweat flowing from your body is the sea of your earth, your hair is the vegetation of your earth, your limbs are the trees of your earth, and so on for all your parts. So when the pillars of your body collapse in death, then the earth has quaked its quaking. When the bones separate from the flesh, then the earth and the mountains are borne and crushed with a single crushing. When the bones are scattered, then the mountains are blown away as dust. When your heart darkens at death, then the sun is coiled up. When your hearing, sight, and all your senses cease, then the stars are dimmed. When your brain splits, then the sky is rent asunder. When sweat bursts from your brow from the terror of death, then the seas are made to gush forth. When one of your legs twists around the other—and they are your mounts—then the pregnant camels are neglected. When the spirit departs the body, then the earth is borne and stretched forth until it casts out what is within it and empties itself. I will not prolong with all the balancing of states and terrors, but I say: by mere death, this lesser resurrection rises upon you, and nothing of the greater resurrection that pertains to you escapes you—only what pertains to others. For the persistence of the celestial bodies in relation to others—what benefits you when your senses, by which you benefit from gazing at them, have been scattered? The blind man finds night and day, the eclipse of the sun and its clearing, equal, for it has been eclipsed for him all at once—that is his portion from it. The clearing after that is the portion of another. And for whom his head is split, his sky is rent, for the sky is an expression for what lies in the direction of the head. So he who has no head has no sky; whence then does the persistence of the sky for another benefit him? This is the lesser resurrection, and fear is below and terror is behind. That is when the great catastrophe comes, particularity is lifted, the heavens and the earth are nullified, the mountains are blown away, and terrors grow. Know that this lesser one, though we have elaborated in describing it, we have not mentioned a tenth of its descriptions. And it, in relation to the greater resurrection, is like the minor birth in relation to the major birth. For man has two births: one is the emergence from the loins and ribs into the repository of wombs. He is in the womb in a secure lodging for a known term, and in his journey to completion he has stages and phases—from a drop, to a clinging clot, to a chewed lump, and others—until he emerges from the narrowness of the womb into the expanse of the world. The proportion of the universality of the greater resurrection to the particularity of the lesser resurrection is like the proportion of the vastness of the world's expanse to the vastness of the womb's expanse. And the proportion of the vastness of the world which the servant advances upon at death to the vastness of the worldly expanse is also like the proportion of the worldly expanse to the womb—indeed, wider and greater. So judge the Hereafter by the first. Your creation and your resurrection are but as a single soul. The second genesis is only according to the measure of the first genesis. Nay, the number of geneses is not confined to two, and to this is the allusion in His saying, exalted is He: "And We shall bring you forth in a fashion you do not know." So he who affirms the two resurrections is a believer in the unseen and the seen, and convinced of the dominion and the sovereignty. He who affirms the lesser resurrection but not the greater is looking with a defective eye at one of the two worlds, and that is ignorance and misguidance, and following the one-eyed deceiver. How great is your heedlessness, O wretched one—and all of us are that wretched one—while these terrors are before you. If you, out of ignorance and misguidance, do not believe in the greater resurrection, does not the indication of the lesser resurrection suffice you? Or have you not heard the saying of the master of the prophets: "Sufficient is death as an admonisher"? Or have you not heard how he, peace be upon him, would turn pale at death until he said: "O Allah, make the pangs of death easy for Muhammad"? Or are you not ashamed of your slowness in anticipating death's assault, following the heedless rabble who look not except for a single blast that seizes them while they are disputing, so they can make no bequest, nor to their people do they return? Then illness comes to them as a warner from death, yet they take no warning; and grey hair comes to them as a messenger from it, yet they reflect not. So, alas for the servants! There comes not to them a messenger but they mock him. Do they think they will be immortal in this world? Or have they not seen how many generations We destroyed before them? They will not return to them. Or do they reckon that the dead have journeyed away from them, so they are deprived? Nay! Verily, all are gathered.

We have them present, yet whenever a sign from their Lord's signs comes to them, they turn away from it. This is because We have placed a barrier before them and a barrier behind them, and We have covered them, so they do not see. It is the same to them whether you warn them or do not warn them—they will not believe. Let us return to the purpose, for these are allusions pointing to matters higher than the sciences of transaction. We say: It has become clear that patience is an expression for the steadfastness of the motive of religion in resisting the motive of base desire. This resistance is a particular characteristic of human beings, due to the Noble Recorders assigned to them, for they do not record anything concerning children or the insane, as we have mentioned that the good deed lies in turning towards benefiting from them, and the sin lies in turning away from them. Children and the insane have no means to benefit, so turning towards or away is not conceivable from them, and they only record the turning towards and turning away from those capable of it. By my life, indeed the beginnings of the illumination of the light of guidance may appear at the age of discernment and grow gradually until the age of maturity, just as the light of dawn appears until the sun's disc rises. But this is a limited guidance that does not lead to the harms of the Hereafter, only to the harms of this world. Therefore, one is struck immediately for neglecting prayers, but is not punished for neglecting them in the Hereafter, nor is anything written on his scrolls to be unfolded in the Hereafter. Rather, it is upon the just guardian and the compassionate, righteous protector—if he is among the righteous and follows the conduct of the Noble, Righteous, Good Recorders—to write the child's good and evil deeds upon the scroll of his heart. He writes it upon him through preservation, then unfolds it upon him through recognition, then punishes him for it with a strike. Every guardian who follows this conduct regarding the child has indeed inherited the character of the angels and employed it regarding the child, thereby attaining a degree of nearness to the Lord of the Worlds, just as the angels attained it. Thus, he will be with the Prophets, those brought near, and the truthful. To this, the allusion is in His saying, peace be upon him: "I and the guardian of the orphan will be like these two in Paradise," and he pointed to his two noble fingers, peace be upon him.

(Explanation that Patience is Half of Faith)
Know that faith is sometimes specifically used to denote affirmation of the fundamentals of religion, sometimes specifically for righteous actions emanating from it, and sometimes it denotes both together. There are chapters for knowledge and chapters for actions. Since the term 'faith' encompasses all of them, faith comprises more than seventy chapters. We have mentioned the differences in these usages in the book 'The Foundations of Beliefs' from the quarter of worship. However, patience is half of faith based on two considerations and according to two usages. The first is that it denotes both affirmations and actions together, so faith has two pillars: one is certainty, and the other is patience. What is meant by certainty is the definitive knowledge attained by God Almighty guiding His servant to the fundamentals of religion. What is meant by patience is acting according to the requirement of certainty, for certainty informs him that disobedience is harmful and obedience is beneficial, and it is impossible to abandon disobedience and persist in obedience except through patience, which is employing the motive of religion to overcome the motive of base desire and laziness. Thus, patience is half of faith by this consideration. For this reason, the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, combined them, saying: "Among the least you have been given is certainty and the resolve of patience," and the rest of the hadith. The second consideration is that it denotes the states that yield actions, not the knowledge. At that point, all that the servant encounters is divided into what benefits him in this world and the Hereafter or harms him in both. In relation to what harms him, the state is patience; in relation to what benefits him, the state is gratitude. Thus, gratitude becomes one of the two halves of faith by this consideration, just as certainty is one of the two halves by the first consideration. With this perspective, Ibn Mas'ud, may God be pleased with him, said: "Faith is two halves: half patience and half gratitude." It has also been attributed to the Messenger of God, peace be upon him. Since patience is patience from the motive of base desire through the steadfastness of the motive of religion, and the motive of base desire is of two kinds: a motive from the side of lust, and a motive from the side of anger—lust being for seeking pleasure and anger for fleeing from pain—and since fasting is patience from the requirement of lust only, which is the lust of the stomach and private parts, not from the requirement of anger, it is said by this consideration: "Fasting is half of patience." Because the perfection of patience lies in patience from the incitements of lust and the incitements of anger together, thus fasting by this consideration becomes a quarter of faith. This is how one should understand the estimations of the Sacred Law regarding the limits of actions and states and their relation to faith. The foundation of this is to know the numerous chapters of faith, for the name 'faith' is used in different ways.

(Explanation of the Names that are Renewed for Patience in Relation to What One is Patient About)
Know that patience is of two kinds: One is a physical kind, such as enduring hardships with the body and remaining steadfast upon them.

It is either through action, such as undertaking arduous works—be they acts of worship or otherwise—or through endurance, such as patience under severe beating, grave illness, and grievous wounds. This may be praiseworthy if it conforms to the Sacred Law, but the complete and praiseworthy form is the other kind, which is the patience of the soul in abstaining from the desires of natural disposition and the demands of caprice. This latter kind, if it is patience against the appetites of the belly and the private parts, is called chastity. If it is in enduring something disliked, its names among people differ according to the disliked thing over which patience prevails. If it is in a calamity, it is confined to the name 'patience,' and its opposite state is called agitation and panic, which is to let the caller of caprice run loose in raising the voice, striking the cheeks, tearing the collars, and the like. If it is in enduring wealth, it is called self-restraint, and its opposite state is called insolence. If it is in war and combat, it is called courage, and its opposite is cowardice. If it is in suppressing rage and anger, it is called forbearance, and its opposite is complaint. If it is in a vexing tribulation of time, it is called breadth of the chest, and its opposite is weariness, annoyance, and narrowness of the chest. If it is in concealing speech, it is called keeping a secret, and its possessor is called secretive. If it is abstaining from the superfluities of life, it is called asceticism, and its opposite is greed. If it is patience with a meager portion of shares, it is called contentment, and its opposite is voracity. Thus, most of the traits of faith are encompassed within patience. For this reason, when the Prophet (peace be upon him) was once asked about faith, he said, "It is patience," because its acts are many and most noble, as he said, "Perform the pilgrimage to 'Arafah." God, exalted is He, has gathered all these divisions and named the whole 'patience,' saying, exalted is He: "And those who are patient in poverty and hardship and during battle. Those are the ones who have been true, and it is those who are the righteous." Therefore, these are the divisions of patience according to their different objects. He who derives meanings from names may think these states differ in their essences and realities because he sees the names as different. But he who follows the straight path and looks with the light of God, exalted is He, observes the meanings first, thus discerning their realities, then observes the names, for they were coined to indicate meanings. Thus, the meanings are the foundations and the words are the subsidiaries. He who seeks the foundations from the subsidiaries is bound to slip. To these two groups is the allusion in His saying, exalted is He: "Then is he who walks fallen on his face better guided, or he who walks upright on a straight path?" For the disbelievers only erred in what they erred in due to such inversions. We ask God for good success through His generosity and gentleness.

(Explanation of the divisions of patience according to the difference of strength and weakness)
Know that the motivator of religion, in relation to the motivator of caprice, has three states. The first is that it overpowers the caller of caprice, leaving it no strength to contend, and this is reached through constant patience. At this point, it is said, "Whoever is patient, prevails." Those who attain this rank are the fewest; no wonder they are the truthful ones brought near, who said, "Our Lord is God," and then remained steadfast. These have adhered to the straight path and stood firm on the upright way, and their souls have found tranquility in accordance with the demand of the motivator of religion. It is to them the caller calls: "O reassured soul, return to your Lord, well-pleased and pleasing [to Him]." The second state is that the callers of caprice prevail and the contention of the motivator of religion drops completely, so he surrenders himself to the troops of satans and does not struggle due to his despair of struggling. These are the heedless, and they are the majority. They are those whose desires have enslaved them and whose wretchedness has overcome them, so they have given God's enemies authority over their hearts, which are a secret among God's secrets and a matter among God's matters. To them is the allusion in His saying, exalted is He: "And if We had willed, We could have given every soul its guidance, but the word from Me will come true: 'I will surely fill Hell with jinn and people all together.'" These are they who have purchased the worldly life at the expense of the Hereafter, so their transaction has incurred loss. To one who intends to guide them, it is said: "So turn away from he who turns away from Our remembrance and desires not except the worldly life. That is their sum of knowledge." The sign of this state is despair, hopelessness, and delusion by wishes, which is the height of foolishness, as it is said: "The intelligent one is he who holds his soul to account and works for what is after death, and the fool is he who follows his soul its caprice and places hopes in God." The possessor of this state, when admonished, says: "I long for repentance, but it has become impossible for me, so I have no hope in it," or he does not long for repentance but says: "God is Forgiving, Merciful, Generous, so He has no need of my repentance." This wretched one has made his intellect subservient to his desire, so he uses his intellect only to deduce subtle stratagems by which he may attain the fulfillment of his desire. Thus, his intellect has become in the hands of his desires like a Muslim captive in the hands of disbelievers, who employ him for

Tending to swine, safeguarding and transporting wine, and the one who does so occupies with God Almighty the same position as one who subdues a Muslim and delivers him to the disbelievers, making him a captive among them. For by his grievous transgression, he resembles one who has subjugated what by right should not be subjugated and empowered what by right should not be empowered over it. The Muslim is deserving of being the one in authority due to what he possesses of the knowledge of God and the impetus of religion. The disbeliever is deserving of being subjected to authority due to what he possesses of ignorance of religion and the incitement of devils. The right of the Muslim over his own self is more obligatory than the right of others over him. Therefore, whenever the noble faculty, which is from the party of God and the host of angels, is subjugated to the base faculty, which is from the party of devils distanced from God Almighty, it is as if one has enslaved a Muslim for a disbeliever. Nay, it is as if one sought out the king who had bestowed favor upon him, then took his most precious child and delivered him to his most hated enemy. Consider, then, how such an act constitutes ingratitude for His favor and warrants His chastisement. For passion is the most detested deity worshipped on earth in the sight of God Almighty, and intellect is the most honored being created on the face of the earth.

The third state is when the war is seesawing between the two hosts, so at times he has the upper hand over it, and at times it has the upper hand over him. Among the strugglers, his like is counted, not among the triumphant. The people of this state are those who have mixed a righteous deed with another evil one; perhaps God will turn to them in forgiveness. This is with consideration of strength and weakness. Three states also pertain to it with consideration of the number of desires one abstains from. For either he overcomes all desires, or he overcomes none of them, or he overcomes some but not others. Applying the word of God Almighty, "They have mixed a righteous deed with another evil one," to one who is incapable of overcoming some desires but not others is more fitting. Those who abandon struggle against desires entirely are likened to cattle; rather, they are more astray in path, for the beast was not created with the knowledge and capability by which to struggle against the demands of desires, whereas this one was created with that and left it idle. He is truly deficient, assuredly ruined. Thus it has been said:
         "And I have not seen among the faults of people a fault like the deficiency of those capable of perfection."

Patience is also divided, with consideration of ease and difficulty, into that which is hard upon the soul, so that constancy in it is not possible except with immense effort and severe toil—this is called 'forcing patience'—and into that which occurs without severe toil, but is attained with the slightest exertion upon the soul, and this is specifically named 'patience'. When God-consciousness becomes constant and conviction in the ultimate good outcome is strengthened, patience is made easy. Therefore, God Almighty said, "As for he who gives and fears God and believes in the best [reward], We will ease him toward ease." An example of this division is the ability of a wrestler against another. The strong man is able to throw the weak one with the slightest charge and easiest effort, such that he meets no weariness or fatigue in wrestling him, nor is his soul disturbed, nor is he bewildered. But he is not able to throw the powerful one except with toil, increased effort, and sweat upon the brow. Thus is the wrestling between the impetus of religion and the impetus of passion, for in truth it is a wrestling between the hosts of angels and the hosts of devils. Whenever desires submit and are subdued, and the impetus of religion gains mastery and prevails, and patience is eased through prolonged perseverance, that bequeaths the station of contentment, as will come in the Book of Contentment. Thus contentment is higher than patience. Therefore, He (peace be upon him) said, "Worship God in a state of contentment. If you are not able, then in patience over what you dislike there is much good." And one of the knowers said: The people of patience are upon three stations: The first is abandoning desire—this is the degree of the repentant. The second is contentment with what is decreed—this is the degree of the ascetics. The third is love for what one's Master does with him—this is the degree of the truthful. We will clarify in the Book of Love that the station of love is higher than contentment, just as the station of contentment is higher than the station of patience. This division pertains specifically to patience over calamities and tribulations.

Know that patience is also divided, with consideration of its ruling, into obligatory, supererogatory, disliked, and forbidden. Patience in abstaining from prohibitions is obligatory. Patience over disliked matters is supererogatory. Patience in enduring a forbidden harm is forbidden, such as one whose hand or the hand of his child is cut off and he remains patient and silent about it, or one who intends one's protected kin with a forbidden desire, provoking his protective jealousy, yet he patiently refrains from showing his jealousy and remains silent about what befalls his family—this patience is forbidden. The disliked patience is patience in enduring a harm that befalls him through a means disliked in the Sacred Law. Therefore, let the Sacred Law be the criterion for patience. That patience is half of faith should not delude you into thinking all of it is praiseworthy; rather, what is meant by it are specific types of patience.

(Clarification of the situations requiring patience and that the servant cannot do without it in any state)
Know that all that befalls the servant in this life does not escape two types: one is that which conforms to his passion, and the other is that which does not conform to it but is disliked by him. He is in need of patience in each one.

From these two, man in all circumstances is not free from one of these two types or from both. Therefore, he can never do without patience. The first type is that which conforms to desire: namely, health, safety, wealth, status, numerous kin, ample means, many followers and supporters, and all worldly pleasures. How greatly the servant needs patience concerning these matters! For if he does not restrain himself from indulging, relying upon them, and immersing himself in their permissible pleasures, this will lead him to arrogance and transgression. Indeed, man becomes transgressing when he sees himself self-sufficient. Thus, some of the knowers of God have said: "Affliction is borne patiently by the believer, but well-being is not borne patiently except by the sincere." Sahl said: "Patience in well-being is more difficult than patience in affliction." When the doors of the world were opened upon the Companions, may God be pleased with them, they said: "We were tested with the trial of hardship and we were patient, then we were tested with the trial of ease and we were not patient." Therefore, God warned His servants of the trial of wealth, spouse, and children, saying, Exalted is He: "O you who have believed, let not your wealth and your children divert you from the remembrance of God." And He, Mighty and Majestic, said: "Indeed, among your spouses and your children are enemies to you, so beware of them." And the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, said: "A child is a cause of miserliness, cowardice, and grief." And when the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, saw his son al-Hasan, may God be pleased with him, stumbling in his shirt, he descended from the pulpit, embraced him, and said: "God has spoken the truth: 'Your wealth and your children are but a trial.' Verily, when I saw my son stumbling, I could not restrain myself from taking him." In this is a lesson for those of insight. The true man is he who is patient in well-being. The meaning of patience in it is not to rely upon it, knowing that all of it is a trust deposited with him, and it is likely to be reclaimed soon; and not to let himself go in rejoicing over it, nor to immerse himself in enjoyment, pleasure, amusement, and play; and to observe the rights of God in his wealth by spending, in his body by offering help to creation, in his tongue by offering truthfulness, and likewise in all that God has bestowed upon him. This patience is connected to gratitude; it is not complete except by fulfilling the right of gratitude, as will come. Patience in ease is more severe because it is coupled with ability, and part of preservation is not to have power. Patience on being cupped and bled when someone else performs it is easier than patience on bleeding and cupping yourself. The hungry person in the absence of food is more capable of patience than when delicious, appetizing foods are present before him and he has power over them. For this reason, the trial of ease is great.

The second type is that which does not conform to desire and natural disposition. This is not free from either being connected to the servant's choice, like acts of obedience and disobedience, or not being connected to choice, like calamities and adversities, or not being connected to his choice but he has a choice in removing it, like seeking redress from a wrongdoer by taking revenge on him. These are three categories. The first category is what is connected to his choice, which are all his actions described as being obedience or disobedience, and these are two kinds. The first kind: obedience, and the servant needs patience in it. Patience in obedience is difficult because the soul by its nature recoils from servitude and desires lordship. Therefore, some of the knowers said: "There is no soul except that it conceals what Pharaoh manifested from his saying: 'I am your lord, most high.' But Pharaoh found for it an opportunity and acceptance, so he manifested it when he deemed his people weak and they obeyed him. There is no one except that he calls to that with his slave, servant, followers, and everyone under his coercion and obedience, even if he refrains from manifesting it. For his rage and fury when they fall short in his service and enslavement of them issues only from the concealment of arrogance and contesting lordship in the garment of pride." Therefore, servitude is burdensome to the soul absolutely. Then among acts of worship are those disliked due to laziness, like prayer; those disliked due to stinginess, like zakah; and those disliked due to both, like pilgrimage and jihad. Thus, patience in obedience is patience in hardships. The obedient one needs patience in his obedience in three states: The first is before the obedience, which is in correcting the intention, sincerity, and patience from the blemishes of ostentation and the incitements of corruptions, and resolving upon sincerity and fulfillment. This is among the severe patience for one who knows the reality of intention, sincerity, the corruptions of ostentation, and the stratagems of the soul. The Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, alerted to this when he said: "Actions are but by intentions, and every man shall have only what he intended." And God, Exalted is He, said: "And they were not commanded except to worship God, sincere to Him in religion." For this reason, God, Exalted is He, gave precedence to patience over action, saying: "Except for those who are patient and do righteous deeds." The second state is the state of action, so that he is not heedless of God during his action nor lazy in realizing its manners and practices.

And he persists in observing the condition of proper conduct until the final completion of the work, thus adhering to patience against the incitements of lassitude until finishing. This too is among the severe trials of patience, and perhaps it is what is intended by the saying of the Exalted: "Excellent is the reward of the workers, those who are patient" — meaning they are patient until the work is complete. The third state is after finishing the work, for one then needs patience against disclosing it and displaying it for reputation and ostentation, and patience against viewing it with the eye of self-admiration, and against all that would nullify his deed and invalidate its effect, as the Exalted has said: "And do not invalidate your deeds," and as the Exalted has said: "Do not invalidate your charities with reminders of generosity and injury." So whoever is not patient after charity against reminders and injury has indeed nullified his deed. Acts of obedience are divided into obligatory and supererogatory, and one needs patience for both of them. God the Exalted has combined them in His saying: "Indeed, God commands justice, excellence, and giving to relatives." So justice is the obligatory, excellence is the supererogatory, and giving to relatives is chivalry and maintaining kinship ties, all of which require patience. The second category is sins; how greatly the servant needs patience against them! God the Exalted has gathered the types of sins in His saying: "And He forbids indecency, wrong, and oppression," and He said, peace be upon him: "The emigrant is he who abandons evil, and the striver is he who strives against his desire." Sins are the demand of the prompting of desire. The most severe type of patience is patience against sins that have become familiar through habit, for habit is a second nature. When habit is added to appetite, two soldiers from the soldiers of Satan ally against the soldiers of God the Exalted, and the motive of religion is not strong enough to suppress them. Then, if that action is something easy to do, patience against it is heavier upon the soul, such as patience against sins of the tongue like backbiting, lying, argumentation, and praising oneself either indirectly or explicitly, and the types of jesting that hurt hearts, and the kinds of words intended for disparagement and contempt, and mentioning the dead and criticizing them, their knowledge, their conduct, and their positions. For that is outwardly backbiting and inwardly self-praise. The soul has in it two appetites: one is negating others, and the other is affirming itself, and through them is completed for it the lordship that is in its nature, which is contrary to what it has been commanded regarding servitude. Due to the combination of the two appetites, the ease of moving the tongue, and that becoming habitual in conversations, patience against it becomes difficult, and it is among the greatest destructive sins, to the point that its reprehensibility and ugliness are nullified in hearts due to its frequent repetition and widespread familiarity with it. Thus you see a person, for example, wearing silk and finding it extremely objectionable, yet he lets his tongue loose all day long concerning people's honor and does not find that objectionable, despite what has come in the report: "Indeed, backbiting is more severe than fornication." And whoever cannot control his tongue in conversations and is incapable of patience against that, then seclusion and solitude are obligatory for him; nothing else will save him. So patience in solitude is easier than patience in silence while mixing with others. The severity of patience varies among individual sins according to the strength or weakness of the motive for that sin. The easiest of the tongue's movements is the movement of thoughts with varying whisperings. Thus, inevitably, the inner discourse remains in solitude, and it is impossible to have patience against it at all except by overwhelming the heart with another concern in religion that consumes it, like one who wakes with his concerns being a single concern. Otherwise, if he does not employ his thought in something specific, he cannot imagine a cessation of the whispering from it. The second category: that whose assault is not connected to his choice, but he has a choice in repelling it, as when he is harmed by an action or word, and wrong is done to him concerning his person or his wealth. Patience in that, by abandoning retaliation, is sometimes obligatory and sometimes a virtue. Some of the Companions, may God be pleased with them, said: "We did not consider a man's faith to be faith if he did not have patience against harm." And the Exalted said: "And we will surely be patient over whatever harm you cause us, and upon God let the reliant rely." And the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, once distributed some wealth, and a Bedouin among the Muslims said, "This is a distribution not intended for the sake of God." The Messenger of God was informed, and his cheeks reddened, then he said: "May God have mercy on my brother Moses; he was harmed more than this and was patient." And the Exalted said: "And leave their harm and rely upon God." And the Exalted said: "And be patient over what they say and leave them with gracious avoidance." And the Exalted said: "And We already know that your breast is constrained by what they say. So exalt [God] with praise of your Lord." And the Exalted said: "And you will surely hear much abuse from those who were given the Scripture before you and from the polytheists. But if you are patient and fear God – indeed, that is of the matters of resolve" — meaning you are patient against retaliation. For that reason, God the Exalted praised those who pardon their rights in retribution and otherwise, saying: "And if you punish..."

So repay with the like of what you were afflicted with, but if you are patient, it is indeed better for the patient ones. And it is said: "Reconcile with the one who cuts you off, give to the one who deprives you, and pardon the one who wrongs you." I saw in the Gospel that Jesus, son of Mary, peace be upon him, said: "It was said to you of old: 'An eye for an eye and a tooth for a tooth.' But I say to you: Do not resist evil. Rather, whoever strikes you on the right cheek, turn to him the other also. And whoever takes your cloak, give him your tunic as well. And whoever compels you to go one mile, go with him two." All of this is a command to be patient with harm, for patience with the harm of people is among the highest ranks of patience, because in it the motive of religion and the motive of desire and anger all cooperate.

The third category is that which does not fall under the scope of choice, neither at its beginning nor its end, such as calamities: the death of loved ones, the loss of wealth, the removal of health through illness, blindness of the eye, corruption of the limbs, and in summary, all types of affliction. Patience upon that is among the highest stations of patience. Ibn Abbas, may God be pleased with them both, said: "Patience in the Quran is of three kinds: Patience in performing the obligations of God Almighty, for which there are three hundred degrees; patience in abstaining from the prohibitions of God Almighty, for which there are six hundred degrees; and patience upon a calamity at the moment of the first shock, for which there are nine hundred degrees." This rank is superior, even though it is from the virtues, to what preceded it, which is from the obligations, because every believer is capable of patience in abstaining from prohibitions. As for patience upon the affliction of God Almighty, none can bear it except the Prophets, for it is the merchandise of the truthful ones, for that is severe upon the soul. Therefore, it is said: "I ask You for such certainty that the calamities of this world are made light for me through it." This is patience whose foundation is sound certainty.

Abu Sulayman said: "By God, we are not patient with what we love, so how can we be patient with what we dislike?" And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: "God Almighty said: 'When I direct a calamity to one of My servants concerning his body, his wealth, or his child, and he meets it with beautiful patience, I will be ashamed on the Day of Resurrection to set up a scale for him or to open a record for him.'" And he said: "Awaiting relief with patience is an act of worship." And he said: "There is no believing servant afflicted by a calamity who says, as God Almighty has commanded: 'Indeed we belong to God, and indeed to Him we return. O God, reward me for my calamity and give me better than it,' except that God will do that for him."

And Anas reported to me that the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: "God Almighty said: 'O Gabriel, what is the reward for one whose two precious ones I have taken?' He said: 'Glory be to You, we have no knowledge except what You have taught us.' God Almighty said: 'His reward is abiding in My abode and gazing upon My Countenance.'"

And he said: "God Almighty says: 'When I test My servant with an affliction and he is patient and does not complain of Me to his visitors, I replace his flesh with flesh better than his flesh and his blood with blood better than his blood. And when I heal him, I heal him with no sin upon him. And if I cause him to die, then he is to My mercy.'"

And David, peace be upon him, said: "O Lord, what is the reward of the sorrowful one who is patient with calamities seeking Your pleasure?" He said: "His reward is that I clothe him in the garment of faith and never remove it from him."

And Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, may God have mercy on him, said in his sermon: "God has not bestowed a blessing upon a servant, then taken it from him and compensated him with patience for it, except that what He compensated him with was better than what He took from him." And he recited: "Indeed, the patient will be given their reward without account."

And Fudayl was asked about patience. He said: "It is contentment with the decree of God." It was said: "How is that?" He said: "The contented one does not wish for a station above his own."

And it is said that al-Shibli, may God have mercy on him, was confined in the hospital. A group entered upon him. He said: "Who are you?" They said: "Your lovers, we have come to visit you." He began to throw stones at them, and they fled. He said: "If you were my lovers, you would have been patient with my affliction."

And one of the knowers had a note in his pocket. He would take it out every hour and look at it. On it was written: "And be patient for the decree of your Lord, for indeed, you are in Our eyes."

And it is said that a woman from the family of al-Fath al-Mawsili stumbled and her fingernail was torn off. She laughed. It was said to her: "Do you not feel the pain?" She said: "The delight of its reward has removed from my heart the bitterness of its pain."

And David said to Solomon, peace be upon them both: "The piety of the believer is known by three things: good reliance upon God for what has not been attained, good contentment with what has been attained, and good patience for what has passed."

And our Prophet, peace be upon him, said: "From honoring God and knowing His right is that you do not complain of your pain nor mention your calamity."

And it is narrated from one of the righteous that he went out one day with a purse in his sleeve, then missed it, finding it had been taken from his sleeve. He said: "May God bless him in it; perhaps he is in greater need of it than I."

And it is narrated from one of them that he said: "I passed by Salim, the freed slave of Abu Hudhayfah, among the slain, and he had a last breath. I said to him: 'Shall I give you water to drink?' He said: 'Drag me a little toward the enemy and put the water in the shield, for I am fasting. If I live until night, I will drink it.' Thus was the patience of those who tread the path of the Hereafter upon the affliction of God Almighty."

If you ask: "How does one attain the rank of patience in afflictions when the matter is not within one's choice, for one is compelled, willing or unwilling? If what is intended is that there be no dislike of the affliction in one's soul, that is not within one's choice." Then know that one only departs from the station of the patient through despair, tearing collars, striking cheeks, excessive complaint, displaying gloom, and altering one's customary dress, furnishings, and food. These matters are within one's choice, so one must avoid them all, show contentment with the decree of God Almighty, remain steadfast in one's usual practice, and believe that it was a trust that has been reclaimed. As narrated from al-Rumaysa', the mother of Sulaym, may God have mercy on her, who said: "A son of mine died while my husband Abu Talha was absent. I arose, shrouded him, and placed him in a corner of the house. Abu Talha arrived, so I arose and prepared his breakfast. He began to eat and said, 'How is the boy?' I said, 'In the best state, praise be to God.' For indeed, since he fell ill, he has not been quieter than this night.' Then I adorned myself for him in the best manner I used to adorn myself before that, until he fulfilled his need from me. Then I said, 'Are you not amazed by our neighbors?' He said, 'What about them?' I said, 'They lent something on loan, and when it was requested back and reclaimed, they despaired.' He said, 'How evil is what they did!' I said, 'This son of yours was a trust from God Almighty, and God has taken him back.' So he praised God and said, 'To God we belong and to Him we return.' Then he went in the morning to the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, and informed him. He said, 'O God, bless them in their night.'" The narrator said: "I later saw for them in the mosque seven, all of whom had recited the Qur'an." And Jabir narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: "I saw myself entering Paradise, and there I was with al-Rumaysa', the wife of Abu Talha." And it has been said: "Beautiful patience is that the one afflicted is not distinguished from others, nor does heartfelt grief or the overflowing of tears remove him from the bounds of the patient," since that occurs equally among all present due to death, and because weeping is heartfelt grief for the deceased, for that is a requirement of human nature and does not leave a person until death. Thus, when Ibrahim, the son of the Prophet, peace be upon him, died, his eyes overflowed, and it was said to him: "Did you not forbid us from this?" He said: "This is mercy, and God only shows mercy to the merciful among His servants." Rather, that also does not remove one from the station of contentment. For one who undergoes cupping or bloodletting is content with it, yet is inevitably pained by it, and his eyes may overflow if his pain is severe. This will come, God willing, in the Book of Contentment. And Ibn Abi Najih wrote, consoling one of the caliphs: "The most deserving to recognize the right of God Almighty in what He has taken is one who recognizes the greatness of God's right upon him in what He has left for him. And know that what has passed before you is what remains for you, and what remains after you is what is rewarded for you." And know that the reward of the patient for what befalls them is greater than the blessing upon them for what they are spared from. Therefore, whenever one repels dislike by reflecting on God Almighty's favor upon him through reward, he attains the rank of the patient. Indeed, part of the perfection of patience is concealing illness, poverty, and all afflictions. It has been said: "Among the treasures of righteousness are concealing afflictions and pains, and charity." Thus, it has become clear to you through these divisions that the obligation of patience is general in all states and actions. For he who has restrained all appetites and secluded himself alone cannot do without patience in solitude and isolation outwardly, and patience against the whisperings of Satan inwardly. For the agitation of thoughts does not subside, and most wandering of thoughts is either about a past that cannot be recovered or a future from which what is decreed must inevitably occur. Thus, however it may be, it is a waste of the servant's time, whose instrument is his heart and whose capital is his lifespan. If the heart is heedless for a single moment from a remembrance by which it gains intimacy with God Almighty, or from a reflection by which it gains knowledge of God Almighty to thereby gain love of God Almighty through knowledge, then it is cheated. This is if his thought and whisperings are confined to permissible matters, but that is not usually the case. Rather, he reflects on devising means to fulfill appetites, for he continually contends with everyone who acts contrary to his desire throughout his entire life, or with whom he imagines contends and opposes his command or desire by showing some sign from him. Indeed, he anticipates opposition from the most sincere people in his love, even from his family and children, and imagines their opposition to him. Then he reflects on how to rebuke them, how to overpower them, and their reply to what they excuse themselves with in opposing him. And he remains constantly occupied. For Satan has two armies: an army that flies and an army that walks. Whispering is the movement of his flying army, and appetite is the movement of his walking army. This is because Satan was created from fire, and man was created from clay like pottery. Pottery combines within it clay and fire. Clay's nature is stillness, and fire's nature is movement. Thus, one cannot conceive of a burning fire that does not move; rather, it continually moves by its nature. And the accursed one, created from fire, has been tasked to find rest.

Regarding his movement in prostration when God created from clay, he refused, grew arrogant, and rebelled, expressing the reason for his rebellion by saying: "You created me from fire and created him from clay." Therefore, since the accursed one did not prostrate to our father Adam—upon him be prayers and peace—it is not fitting to hope for his prostration to Adam's children. Whenever he refrains from the heart's whisperings, aggression, fluttering, and wandering, he has indeed shown submission and compliance. His submission through compliance is a prostration from him; it is the spirit of prostration. Placing the forehead on the ground is merely its mold and its conventional indicative sign. If placing the forehead on the ground were taken as a sign of disrespect by convention, it could be imagined as such, just as lying prostrate before a revered and honored person is seen as disrespect by custom. So let not the shell of the essence distract you from the essence, nor the mold of the spirit from the spirit, nor the husk of the core from the core, lest you be among those whom the realm of the visible completely confines from the realm of the unseen. Realize that Satan is among those granted respite; he will not humble himself to you by ceasing whisperings until the Day of Judgment, unless you become such that your concerns are a single concern, occupying your heart with God alone, leaving the accursed one no avenue within you. Then you will be among God's sincere servants, included in the exception from the dominion of this vile one. Do not think that an empty heart is free from him; rather, he flows through the son of Adam like blood flows, and his flow is like air in a cup. If you wish the cup to be empty of air without filling it with water or something else, you have hoped for the impossible. For as much as it empties of water, air inevitably enters. Similarly, a heart occupied with a significant religious thought is not free from Satan's wandering; otherwise, whoever is heedless of God Almighty, even for a moment, has in that moment no companion but Satan. Thus, God Almighty said: "And whoever turns away from the remembrance of the Most Merciful, We appoint for him a devil, and he is to him a companion." And He said—glory to Him—: "Indeed, God Almighty despises the idle youth." This is because when a youth is idle from work that occupies his inner self with something permissible to aid him in his religion, he appears idle, but his heart is not empty; rather, Satan nests in it, lays eggs, hatches them, then his offspring also mate, lay eggs again, and hatch, and thus Satan's progeny multiplies faster than that of other animals, for his nature is from fire, and when he finds dry tinder, his multiplication increases. Fire continually begets fire without ceasing, spreading gradually and continuously. Desire in the youth's soul is for Satan like dry tinder for fire, and just as fire does not remain if it has no fuel—which is firewood—so Satan has no opportunity if there is no desire. Therefore, if you reflect, you know that your fiercest enemy is your desire, which is an attribute of your soul. Hence, Husayn ibn Mansur al-Hallaj, when he was crucified and asked what Sufism is, said: "It is your soul; if you do not occupy it, it occupies you." Thus, the reality and perfection of patience is patience from every blameworthy movement, and the movement of the inner self is more deserving of patience from that. This is a constant patience, severed only by death. We ask God for good success through His grace and generosity.

(Explanation of the Remedy of Patience and What Aids in It)
Know that He who sent down the ailment also sent down the remedy and promised healing. So patience, even if difficult or seemingly impossible, is attainable through the mixture of knowledge and action. Knowledge and action are the humors from which remedies for all heart ailments are compounded, but each ailment requires different knowledge and different action. And just as the types of patience vary, the types of impediments preventing it vary, and when the ailments differ, the treatment differs, for the meaning of treatment is opposing the ailment and suppressing it. Exhausting this would be lengthy, but we indicate the way through some examples. We say: If one needs patience from the desire for intercourse, for example, and desire has overpowered him such that he cannot control his private part, or he can control his private part but cannot control his eye, or he can control his eye but cannot control his heart and soul, as they continually prompt him according to desires, diverting him from consistency in remembrance, reflection, and righteous deeds. We say: We have previously stated that patience is an expression of the struggle between the impulse of religion and the impulse of desire. Whenever two contenders struggle, if we want one to overcome the other, we have no way but to strengthen the one we want to have the upper hand and weaken the other. Thus, here we must strengthen the impulse of religion and weaken the impulse of desire. As for the impulse of desire, the way to weaken it is three matters. First: Look to the substance of its strength, which is delicious foods that stir desire, both in type and quantity. It is necessary to cut them off through constant fasting, coupled with moderation when breaking the fast with little food that is inherently weak in kind, avoiding...

Meat and foods that stimulate desire. The second: cutting off its immediate stimulating causes, for it is only aroused by looking at the sources of desire, since the gaze stirs the heart, and the heart stirs desire. This is achieved through seclusion and guarding against the places where the sight might fall upon desired images, and fleeing from them entirely. The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: "A glance is an arrow from the arrows of Satan," and it is an arrow that the accursed one aims, and there is no shield to protect from it except closing the eyelids or fleeing from the direction of its shot, for this arrow is only shot from the bow of images; so if you turn away from the direction of the images, his arrow will not strike you. The third: consoling the self with the permissible of the kind it desires, and that is through marriage, for everything the natural disposition desires, there is among the permissible things of its kind what suffices in place of the forbidden from it. This is the most beneficial treatment for most people, for cutting off nourishment weakens one for all other works, and it may not suppress desire for most men. For this reason, he, peace be upon him, said: "You should marry, and whoever is unable, then upon him is fasting, for fasting is a shield for him." These are three causes. So the treatment: the first, which is cutting off food, resembles cutting off fodder from a refractory beast and a predatory dog so that it weakens and its strength diminishes. The second resembles hiding meat from the dog and concealing barley from the beast so that their inner selves are not stirred by seeing it. The third resembles consoling it with a little of what its nature inclines to, so that it retains enough strength to endure discipline. As for strengthening the motive of religion, it is only through two ways: one is enticing it with the benefits of striving and its fruits in religion and this world, by increasing reflection on the reports we have mentioned regarding the merit of patience and its good outcomes in this world and the hereafter. And in the tradition: "The reward for patience over calamity is greater than what was lost," and that because of it, one is envied for the calamity, for what he lost does not remain with him except for the duration of life, and he gains what remains after his death forever. Whoever exchanges something base for something precious should not grieve for the loss of the base in the moment. This is from the realm of spiritual knowledge, and it is from faith, which at times weakens and at times strengthens. If it strengthens, the motive of religion strengthens and is intensely stirred; if it weakens, it weakens it. The strength of faith is expressed by certainty, which is what moves the resolve of patience, and the least that people are given is certainty and the resolve of patience. The second is to accustom this motive to gradually wrestle with the motive of desire, little by little, until it experiences the delight of overcoming it, thus becoming emboldened against it and its power in wrestling it strengthens. For habituation and practice of difficult works confirm the faculties from which those works issue; thus, the strength of porters, farmers, and fighters increases. In sum, the strength of those who practice difficult works increases over the strength of tailors, perfumers, jurists, and the pious, because their faculties have not been confirmed by practice. So the first treatment resembles enticing the wrestler with a robe of honor upon victory and promising him various honors, as Pharaoh promised his magicians when inciting them against Moses, where he said: "And indeed, you will then be among those brought near." The second resembles accustoming the boy who is intended for wrestling and fighting by engaging in its causes from childhood until he becomes familiar with it and emboldened in it, and his power in it strengthens. Whoever entirely abandons striving through patience weakens in him the motive of religion and cannot overcome desire, even if it weakens. And whoever accustoms himself to oppose desire overcomes it whenever he wills. This is the method of treatment in all types of patience, and it cannot be fully detailed. The most severe of them is restraining the inner self from the whisperings of the soul, and this becomes most severe for one who devotes himself to it by suppressing apparent desires, chooses seclusion, and sits for vigilance, remembrance, and reflection. For the insinuations continually pull him from one side to another, and this has no treatment at all except cutting off all attachments, outwardly and inwardly, by fleeing from family, children, wealth, status, companions, and friends, then secluding oneself in a corner after securing a modest amount of sustenance and being content with it. Yet all that is insufficient unless concerns become a single concern, which is God, exalted is He. Then, when that dominates the heart, it is still insufficient unless it has scope in thought and inner journeying through the kingdom of the heavens and the earth and the wonders of God's creation and all the doors of knowing God, exalted is He, until when that takes possession of his heart, it repels the engagement with the tugging of Satan and his insinuations. If he has no inner journeying, nothing will save him except continuous, regularly arranged litanies in every moment of recitation, remembrances, and prayers. Along with that, he needs to oblige the heart to be present, for inner thought is...

He who immerses his heart beyond the outward litanies, even after doing all that, will not have all his moments secured for him, save for a portion of them. For he is not free, in all his times, from newly occurring incidents that distract him from reflection and remembrance—such as illness, fear, harm from others, or aggression from a companion, since he cannot do without associating with those who assist him in some necessities of livelihood. This is one type of distraction.

As for the second type, it is more essential and of greater necessity than the first: his preoccupation with food, clothing, and the means of subsistence. Preparing these also requires engagement, whether he undertakes it himself or another does it for him; he is not free from a heart preoccupied with whoever manages it. However, after severing all attachments, most of his moments become secure for him, unless a calamity or event befalls him. In those moments, the heart becomes clear, reflection becomes easy for him, and there is unveiled within it from the secrets of God Almighty in the dominion of the heavens and the earth what he could not attain a tenth of in a long period, had his heart been preoccupied with attachments. Attaining this is the utmost of the stations that can be reached through acquisition and effort.

As for the measures of what is unveiled and the extent of what comes from the grace of God Almighty in states and deeds, that proceeds like hunting, and it is according to provision. The effort may be little and the catch great, or the effort may be prolonged and the fortune small. Reliance beyond this striving is upon a pull from the pulls of the All-Merciful, for they equal the works of the two weighty creations (jinn and mankind), and that is not by the choice of the servant. Indeed, the servant's choice lies in exposing himself to that pull by cutting off from his heart the attractions of the world, for one pulled toward the lowest of the low is not drawn to the highest high. Everyone anxious about the world is drawn to it. Thus, severing the attracting attachments is what is meant by His saying (in a hadith qudsi): "Verily, your Lord has breezes in the days of your time; so expose yourselves to them." This is because those breezes and pulls have celestial causes, as God Almighty said: "And in the heaven is your provision and what you are promised" (51:22). This is among the highest types of provision.

Celestial matters are hidden from us, so we do not know when God Almighty facilitates the causes of provision. It is incumbent upon us only to empty the place and await the descent of mercy and the reaching of the book's appointed term—like one who prepares the land, clears it of weeds, and scatters seed in it. All that does not benefit him except with rain, and he does not know when God decrees the causes of rain, except that he trusts in the bounty and mercy of God Almighty that He does not leave a year without rain. Likewise, when a year, month, or day passes without a pull from the pulls or a breeze from the breezes, the servant should have purified the heart from the weeds of desires, sown within it the seed of intention and sincerity, and exposed it to the blowing winds of mercy. And just as the anticipation of rains strengthens in the times of spring and at the appearance of clouds, so does the anticipation of those breezes strengthen in the noble times and when aspirations gather and hearts assist one another, as on the Day of Arafah, Friday, and the days of Ramadan. For aspirations and breaths are causes, by the decree of God Almighty, to draw forth His mercy, just as rains are drawn forth in times of seeking rain. They are more suited to drawing forth the rains of unveilings and subtleties of knowledge from the treasuries of the dominion than to drawing forth droplets of water and gathering clouds from the corners of mountains and seas.

Rather, the states and unveilings are present with you in your heart, but you are distracted from them by your attachments and desires, so that becomes a veil between you and them. You need only for the desire to be broken and the veil lifted, then the lights of knowledge shine from the inward heart.
