They would witness the criminals who were being executed, had their limbs amputated, and were tortured with various punishments, in order to thank God Almighty for His protection from such crimes and those penalties, and to thank Him for the blessing of security. They would attend the graveyards and learn that the dearest thing to the dead would be to return to this world, even for a single day. As for the one who disobeyed God Almighty, let him seek to make amends; and as for the one who obeyed, let him increase in his obedience. For the Day of Resurrection is the Day of Mutual Loss; the obedient one is at a loss when he sees the reward for his obedience and says, "I was capable of more than these acts of obedience, so how great is my loss for having wasted some of my time in permissible matters!" As for the disobedient one, his loss is evident. When he observes the graves and learns that the dearest thing to them is to have remaining from their lifespan what remains for him, he should spend the remainder of his life on that for which the inhabitants of the graves desire to return, so that this becomes a recognition of God Almighty's blessings in the remainder of his life—indeed, in the respite granted with every single breath. When he recognizes that blessing, he gives thanks by spending his life on that for which life was created: provisioning from this world for the Hereafter. This is the remedy for these heedless hearts, so that they may feel the blessings of God Almighty, and perhaps they will give thanks. Indeed, Rabi‘ ibn Khuthaym, despite his complete insight, would seek aid through this method to confirm his recognition. He had dug a grave in his house, would place a collar around his neck, and sleep in his grave. Then he would say, "My Lord, send me back, that I might do righteous deeds." Then he would rise and say, "O Rabi‘, you have been given what you asked for, so act before you ask to return, lest you be sent back and not." Among the things by which hearts distant from gratitude should be treated is to know that a blessing, if not thanked for, will depart and not return. For this reason, al-Fudayl ibn ‘Iyad, may God have mercy on him, used to say, "You must adhere to gratitude for blessings, for rarely does a blessing depart from a people and then return to them." Some of the pious predecessors said, "Blessings are wild; so restrain them with gratitude." And in the report: "Whenever God Almighty's blessing upon a servant becomes great, the needs of people toward him increase." So whoever is negligent toward them exposes that blessing to removal. And God, glorified and exalted, says: "Indeed, God will not change the condition of a people until they change what is in themselves." This completes this pillar. The third pillar from the Book of Patience and Gratitude concerns what is shared between patience and gratitude and how they are interconnected.

(Clarifying the Manner in Which Patience and Gratitude Converge Upon a Single Matter)

Perhaps you will say: What you have mentioned regarding blessings indicates that God Almighty has a blessing in every existent thing, and this suggests that tribulation has no existence at all. So what, then, is the meaning of patience? And if tribulation does exist, what is the meaning of gratitude for tribulation? Some claimants have asserted that we give thanks for tribulation, let alone for blessings. So how can gratitude for tribulation be conceived, and how can one give thanks for that upon which one must be patient? Patience in tribulation necessitates pain, while gratitude necessitates joy, and the two are opposites. And what is the meaning of your statement that God Almighty has a blessing upon His servants in everything He has brought into existence? Know that tribulation exists just as blessings exist. The assertion of the affirmation of blessings necessitates the assertion of the affirmation of tribulation, for they are opposites. Thus, the absence of tribulation is a blessing, and the absence of a blessing is a tribulation. However, it has preceded that blessings are divided into an absolute blessing from every aspect—such as in the Hereafter, like the servant's bliss in dwelling in the proximity of God Almighty, and in this world, like faith, good character, and what aids in them—and into a restricted blessing, from one aspect and not another, like wealth which is sound for religion from one aspect and corrupts it from another. Likewise, tribulation is divided into absolute and restricted. As for the absolute in the Hereafter, it is distance from God Almighty, either for a period or forever. As for in this world, it is disbelief, disobedience, and bad character, which lead to absolute tribulation. As for the restricted, it is like poverty, illness, fear, and all other types of tribulation that are not tribulation in religion but in worldly life. Absolute gratitude is for absolute blessings. As for absolute tribulation in this world, one may not be commanded to have patience upon it, for disbelief is a tribulation and there is no meaning to patience upon it; likewise for disobedience. Rather, it is the right of the disbeliever to abandon his disbelief, and likewise the right of the disobedient. Yes, a disbeliever may not know he is a disbeliever, being like one with an ailment who feels no pain due to unconsciousness or the like, so there is no patience upon it. The disobedient one knows he is disobedient, so upon him is to abandon disobedience. Indeed, for every tribulation that a person is capable of repelling, he is not commanded to have patience upon it. For if...

When a person refrains from water despite prolonged thirst until his suffering becomes severe, he is not commanded to endure it patiently; rather, he is commanded to remove the pain. Patience is only for pain that the servant cannot remove. Therefore, patience in this world pertains to what is not an absolute affliction, for it may be a blessing in one respect. Thus, it is conceivable that the duties of patience and gratitude converge upon it. For instance, wealth may become a cause of a person’s ruin—so much so that he is targeted because of what he possesses and is killed, and his children are killed. Health is likewise: there is no worldly blessing among these blessings that cannot potentially become an affliction, though relative to the individual. Similarly, there is no affliction that cannot potentially become a blessing, but relative to a particular state. For there may be a servant for whom what is best lies in poverty and illness; were his body healthy and his wealth abundant, he would become arrogant and transgress. God Almighty has said: “If God were to expand provision for His servants, they would surely transgress on earth” (42:27). And He said: “No indeed! Truly man becomes insolent when he considers himself self-sufficient” (96:6–7). And He said—glory be to Him: “Indeed, God protects His believing servant from the world while He loves him, just as one of you protects his sick one.” Likewise, a wife, child, relative, and everything we have mentioned among the sixteen categories of blessings—except faith and good character—can be imagined to be an affliction for some people, so their opposites would then be blessings for them. For it was previously established that knowledge is a perfection and a blessing, as it is an attribute of God Almighty. Yet it may be an affliction upon the servant in certain matters, and its absence a blessing. An example is a person’s ignorance of his appointed term, for that is a blessing upon him; were he to know it, his life might become bitter and his grief prolonged thereby. Similarly, his ignorance of what his acquaintances and relatives harbor against him is a blessing upon him; were the veil lifted and he became aware of it, his pain, resentment, envy, and preoccupation with retaliation would increase. Likewise, his ignorance of the blameworthy traits in others is a blessing upon him; were he to know them, he would despise and harm them, and that would be a calamity for him in this world and the hereafter. Indeed, even his ignorance of the praiseworthy traits in others may be a blessing upon him, for perhaps the other is a saint of God Almighty, and he is compelled to harm and insult him. If he knew that and still harmed him, his sin would undoubtedly be greater; for one who harms a prophet or a saint while knowing is not like one who harms while not knowing. Among these is God Almighty’s obscuring of the matter of the Resurrection, His obscuring of the Night of Decree, the hour on Friday, and His obscuring of some major sins—all of that is a blessing, because this ignorance intensifies your motives for seeking and striving. These are aspects of God Almighty’s blessings in ignorance, so what about in knowledge? Since we have said that God Almighty has a blessing in every existent thing, that is true, and it applies to everyone without exception by mere conjecture—except the pains He creates in some people. These too may be a blessing for the one suffering them. If they are not a blessing for him, such as the pain resulting from sin, like cutting one’s own hand or tattooing one’s skin, for he suffers from it while being disobedient in it, and the pain of the disbelievers in the Fire—that is also a blessing, but for others among the servants, not for them, because the misfortunes of some people are benefits for others. Were it not that God Almighty created punishment and punished a group with it, those enjoying comfort would not recognize the value of His blessings, even if their joy in them increased. Thus, the joy of the people of Paradise multiplies when they reflect upon the pains of the people of Hell. Do you not see that the people of this world do not greatly rejoice in the light of the sun, despite their intense need for it, because it is universal and freely given? Nor do they greatly rejoice in gazing at the adornment of the heavens, though it is more beautiful than any garden they strive to cultivate on earth. But because the adornment of the heavens is universal, they do not perceive it and do not rejoice because of it. Therefore, what we have mentioned is confirmed: that God Almighty did not create anything except that there is wisdom in it, and He did not create anything except that there is a blessing in it—either for all His servants or for some of them. Hence, in God Almighty’s creation of affliction there is also a blessing, either for the afflicted one or for others. Therefore, every state cannot be described as an absolute affliction nor an absolute blessing, so upon the servant in it converge two duties: patience and gratitude together.

If you say: “But they are opposites; how can they converge, for there is no patience except upon grief, and no gratitude except upon joy?” Know that a single thing may cause grief from one aspect and joy from another, so patience is from the aspect of grief and gratitude from the aspect of joy. And in every poverty, illness, fear, and affliction in this world, there are five matters about which the heedless ought to rejoice and give thanks. First: every calamity and illness—it can be imagined to be greater than it is, for the decreed things of God Almighty are limitless. Had God Almighty weakened it and increased it, what could repel or prevent it? So let him give thanks that it was not greater in this world. Second: it could have been that his calamity was in his religion. A man said to Sahl—may God be pleased with him—

He, exalted be He, said: A thief entered my house and took my belongings, so I said: Give thanks to God, exalted be He, for if Satan had entered your heart and corrupted your monotheism, what would you have done? For this reason, Jesus, peace be upon him, sought refuge in his supplication when he said: O God, do not make my affliction in my religion. And 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, may God be pleased with him, said: I have never been afflicted with a trial except that God, exalted be He, had four blessings upon me therein: that it was not in my religion, that it was not greater than it was, that I was not deprived of contentment with it, and that I hope for reward for it. One of the possessors of hearts had a friend whom the ruler imprisoned. He sent to him informing him and complaining to him, so he said to him: Give thanks to God. He struck him, so he sent to him informing him and complaining to him, so he said: Give thanks to God. Then a Magian was brought and imprisoned with him, and he had a stomach ailment. He was shackled, and a ring from his shackle was placed on his leg and a ring on the leg of the Magian. He sent to him, so he said: Give thanks to God. The Magian needed to get up many times, and he needed to get up with him and stand by his head until he relieved himself. He wrote to him about that, so he said: Give thanks to God. He said: Until when is this, and what trial is greater than this? He said: If the girdle that is around his waist were placed around your waist, what would you have done? Therefore, there is no person afflicted with a trial except that if he were to contemplate sincerely the deficiency of his conduct, outwardly and inwardly, toward his Master, he would see that he deserves more than what he has been afflicted with, in this world and the next. And he who deserves that you be struck one hundred lashes, but he limits it to ten, is deserving of thanks. And he who deserves that your hands be cut off, but he leaves one of them, is deserving of thanks. For this reason, a certain shaykh was passing in a street when a basin of ashes was poured on his head, so he prostrated to God, exalted be He, a prostration of thanks. He was asked: What is this prostration? He said: I was expecting that fire would be poured on me, so the limitation to ashes is a blessing. And it was said to one of them: Do not go out to pray for rain, for the rains have been withheld. He said: You consider the rain delayed, and I consider the stones delayed.

If you say: How can I rejoice when I see a group of people whose disobedience is greater than mine, yet they are not afflicted with what I have been afflicted with, even the disbelievers? Then know that the disbeliever has stored up for him what is greater, and he is only given respite so that he may increase in sin and his punishment be prolonged, as God, exalted be He, said: "We only give them respite that they may increase in sin." As for acts of disobedience, how do you know that in the world there is one who is more disobedient than him? Perhaps a thought of improper conduct toward God, exalted be He, and His attributes is greater and more grievous than drinking wine, fornication, and all other sins of the limbs. For this reason, God, exalted be He, said concerning its like: "And you consider it trivial, while with God it is grave." So how do you know that another is more disobedient than you? Then perhaps his punishment has been delayed to the Hereafter and your punishment hastened in this world, so why do you not thank God, exalted be He, for that? This is the third aspect of thankfulness, which is that there is no punishment except that it is conceivable for it to be delayed to the Hereafter. The afflictions of this world are consoled by other means that lessen the affliction, so its impact is lightened. But the affliction of the Hereafter is everlasting, and if it were not everlasting, there is no way to lighten it by consolation, for the means of consolation are completely cut off in the Hereafter for the tormented. And he whose punishment is hastened in this world is not punished a second time, for the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, said: "When a servant commits a sin and is afflicted with hardship or a trial in this world, God is more generous than to punish him a second time." The fourth is that this affliction and trial were written for him in the Mother of the Book, and it was inevitable that it would reach him. It has reached him, the matter is concluded, and he is relieved of some of it or all of it, so this is a blessing. The fifth is that its reward is greater than it is, for the afflictions of this world are pathways to the Hereafter from two aspects: One is the aspect by which the bitter medicine is a blessing for the sick, and the prevention from means of play is a blessing for the child. For if he were left with play, it would prevent him from knowledge and proper conduct, so he would lose his entire life. Likewise, wealth, family, relatives, and limbs—even the eye, which is the most precious of things—can sometimes be a cause of a person's destruction. Indeed, the intellect, which is the most precious of matters, can be a cause of his destruction. Tomorrow, the atheists will wish they were insane or children and had not used their intellects concerning the religion of God, exalted be He. There is nothing from these means that exists for the servant except that it is conceivable for there to be in it a religious good for him. So he must think well of God, exalted be He, and deem it to contain good and thank Him for it, for the wisdom of God is vast, and He is more knowledgeable of the welfare of His servants than the servants are. Tomorrow, the servants will thank Him for the trials when they see God's reward for the trials, just as the child, after attaining intellect and maturity, thanks his teacher and his father for striking and disciplining him when he perceives the fruit of what he gained from the discipline. And the trial from God, exalted be He, is a discipline.

His care for His servants is more perfect and abundant than the care of parents for their children. It has been narrated that a man said to the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, "Advise me." He said, "Do not suspect God regarding anything He has decreed for you." And he, peace be upon him, looked towards the sky and laughed. When asked, he said, "I marvel at God the Exalted's decree for the believer. If He decrees ease for him, he is content and it is good for him. And if He decrees hardship for him, he is content and it is good for him."

The second aspect is that the chief of destructive sins is love of this world, and the chief cause of salvation is the heart's detachment from the abode of delusion. When blessings come in accordance with one's desire, unmixed with trial and affliction, it causes the heart to become tranquil with this world and its means, and to find comfort in it, until it becomes like Paradise in his sight. Thus, his tribulation at death becomes immense due to his separation from it. When afflictions befall him frequently, his heart is agitated away from the world, finds no rest in it, and takes no comfort in it. It becomes a prison for him, and his salvation from it is the utmost delight, like deliverance from prison. For this reason, he, peace be upon him, said, "The world is the prison of the believer and the paradise of the disbeliever." The disbeliever is everyone who turns away from God the Exalted, desires nothing but the worldly life, is content with it, and finds tranquility in it. The believer is everyone whose heart is severed from the world, intensely yearning to depart from it. Disbelief is partly manifest and partly hidden. According to the measure of love for the world in the heart, hidden polytheism flows within it. Indeed, the absolute monotheist is he who loves nothing but the One True God. Therefore, in affliction there are blessings from this perspective, so one must rejoice in it. As for the feeling of pain, it is inevitable. This is akin to your joy when in need of cupping, at someone who performs your cupping for free, or gives you a beneficial but bitter medicine for free. You feel pain and you rejoice, so you endure the pain and thank him for the cause of joy. Every worldly tribulation is analogous to medicine that pains in the present but benefits in the end. Rather, whoever enters the abode of a king for enjoyment and knows he must inevitably leave it, then sees a beautiful face that will not depart with him from the abode, that is a calamity and tribulation upon him, for it causes him to feel at home in a dwelling where he cannot remain. If, while residing there, there was a danger that the king might discover him and punish him, and then something he disliked befell him that repelled him from staying, that would be a blessing for him. The world is an abode. People entered it through the door of the womb and they will leave it through the door of the grave. Everything that confirms their comfort in the abode is a tribulation, and everything that agitates their hearts away from it and severs their comfort with it is a blessing. Whoever knows this, it is conceivable that he gives thanks for afflictions. Whoever does not recognize these blessings in tribulation, it is inconceivable that he gives thanks, for thanks necessarily follows recognition of the blessing. Whoever does not believe that the reward of the calamity is greater than the calamity itself, it is inconceivable that he gives thanks for the calamity.

It is related that a Bedouin condoled Ibn Abbas for his father, saying:
    Be patient, and we will be patient with you, for indeed
    The patience of the subjects follows the patience of the leader.
    The reward you have after al-Abbas is better than al-Abbas,
    And God is better for al-Abbas than you.
Ibn Abbas said, "No one has condoled me better than his condolence."

The reports concerning patience over calamities are numerous. The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said, "Whomever God desires good for, He afflicts him." And he, peace be upon him, said, "God the Exalted said: 'When I direct a calamity to a servant of Mine concerning his body, his wealth, or his child, and then he meets it with beautiful patience, I would be ashamed on the Day of Resurrection to set up a scale for him or to spread out a record for him.'" And upon him be peace said, "There is no servant afflicted by a calamity who says as God the Exalted has commanded him, 'Indeed we belong to God, and indeed to Him we return. O God, reward me in my calamity and give me better than it,' except that God will do that for him." And he, peace be upon him, said, "God the Exalted said: 'Whoever I deprive of his two precious ones, his reward is eternity in My abode and gazing upon My Countenance.'" And it is narrated that a man said, "O Messenger of God, my wealth is gone and my body is ill." He, peace be upon him, said, "There is no good in a servant whose wealth does not depart and whose body does not become ill. Verily, when God loves a servant, He tests him; and when He tests him, He grants him patience." And the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said, "Verily, a man may have a degree with God the Exalted which he cannot attain by his deeds, until he is tested with a tribulation in his body, and thus attains it by that." And from Khabbab ibn al-Aratt, who said: "We came to the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, while he was reclining on his cloak in the shade of the Kaaba. We complained to him, saying, 'O Messenger of God, will you not call upon God to grant us victory?' He sat up, his face reddened, and then said, 'Those before you would have a man brought, a pit dug for him in the ground, a saw brought and placed on his head, splitting him into two halves, yet that would not turn him away from his religion.'" And from Ali, may God honor his countenance, who said: "Any man imprisoned unjustly by a ruler who then dies is a martyr, and if he is beaten and dies, he is a martyr." And upon him be peace said, "Part of venerating God and knowing His right is that you do not complain of your pain, nor mention..."

Your calamity). Abu al-Dardā’ (may God be pleased with him) said: You are born for death, you build for ruin, you strive for what perishes, and you neglect what endures. How excellent are the three disliked things: poverty, sickness, and death. From Anas, who said: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: "When God the Exalted intends good for a servant and wishes to purify him, He pours tribulation upon him in torrents. When he calls upon Him, the angels say: 'A familiar voice.' And if he calls a second time, saying: 'O Lord,' God the Exalted says: 'Here I am, My servant, and at your service. Ask Me for anything and I will grant it, or I will avert from you what is better, and I have stored for you with Me what is superior to it.' On the Day of Resurrection, the people of deeds will be brought and their deeds weighed in the balance—the people of prayer, fasting, charity, and pilgrimage. Then the people of tribulation will be brought, and no balance will be set up for them, nor any record spread open. Reward will be poured upon them in torrents, just as tribulation was poured upon them in torrents. The people of ease in the world will wish that their bodies had been cut with scissors, when they see what the people of tribulation attain of reward." That is the meaning of His saying, the Exalted: "Indeed, the patient will be given their reward without account." From Ibn ‘Abbās (may God be pleased with them both), who said: A prophet from among the prophets (peace be upon them) complained to his Lord, saying: "O Lord, the believing servant obeys You and avoids Your disobedience, yet the world is withheld from him and tribulation is presented to him. The disbeliever does not obey You and dares to disobey You, yet tribulation is withheld from him and the world is spread out for him." So God the Exalted revealed to him: "The servants and tribulation are Mine. Each glorifies Me with praise. The believer has sins, so I withhold the world from him and present him with tribulation, which becomes an expiation for his sins until he meets Me, and I reward him with his good deeds. The disbeliever has good deeds, so I expand his provision and withhold tribulation from him, and I reward him with his good deeds in the world until he meets Me, and I reward him with his evil deeds." It is related that when the verse was revealed: "Whoever does an evil deed will be recompensed for it," Abu Bakr al-Siddīq (may God be pleased with him) said: "How can one rejoice after this verse?" The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: "May God forgive you, O Abu Bakr. Do you not fall ill? Are you not afflicted with harm? Do you not grieve? This is among that for which you are recompensed," meaning that all that befalls you is an expiation for your sins. From ‘Uqbah ibn ‘Āmir, from the Prophet (peace be upon him), who said: "When you see a man to whom God gives what he loves while he persists in disobedience, know that this is a gradual enticement." Then he recited the saying of the Exalted: "So when they forgot that by which they had been reminded, We opened to them the doors of every thing." Meaning, when they abandoned what they were commanded, We opened to them the doors of good until, when they rejoiced in what they were given—that is, the good they were granted—We seized them suddenly. From al-Hasan al-Basrī (may God have mercy on him): A man from the Companions (may God be pleased with them) saw a woman he knew from the pre-Islamic period and spoke to her, then left her. The man kept glancing back at her as he walked, and he struck a wall, which left a mark on his face. He came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and informed him, and he (peace be upon him) said: "When God intends good for a servant, He hastens the punishment for his sin in this world." And ‘Alī (may God honor his face) said: "Shall I not inform you of the most hopeful verse in the Qur’an?" They said: "Yes." So he recited to them: "And whatever strikes you of disaster—it is for what your hands have earned; and He pardons much." Thus, calamities in the world are due to earning sins. If God punishes him in the world, God is more generous than to punish him a second time. And if He pardons him in the world, God is more generous than to punish him on the Day of Resurrection. From Anas (may God be pleased with him), from the Prophet (peace be upon him), who said: "No servant has ever swallowed two draughts more beloved to God than a draught of anger he restrains with forbearance, and a draught of calamity for which a man is patient. And no drop has ever fallen more beloved to God than a drop of blood shed in the path of God, or a drop of tear in the darkness of the night while he is prostrating and none sees him but God. And no servant has taken two steps more beloved to God the Exalted than a step toward an obligatory prayer and a step toward maintaining family ties." From Abu al-Dardā’, who said: A son of Solomon, son of David (peace be upon them both), died, and he grieved intensely. Two angels came to him, kneeling before him in the guise of disputants. One said: "I sowed seed, and when it ripened, this one passed by and ruined it." He said to the other: "What do you say?" He replied: "I took the path and came upon a crop, looking right and left, and the path was upon it." Solomon (peace be upon him) said: "Why did you sow on the path? Did you not know that people must have a path?" He said: "Then why do you grieve for your son? Did you not know that death is the path to the Hereafter?" So Solomon repented to his Lord and did not grieve for a child after that. ‘Umar ibn ‘Abd al-‘Azīz entered upon a sick son of his and said: "My son, for you to be in my scale is more beloved to me than for me to be in your scale." He said: "O my father, for what you love to be is more beloved to me than..."

that it be what he loves. And from Ibn Abbas, may God be pleased with them both, that the death of his son was announced to him, so he said, "God is the greatest, to God we belong and to Him we return," and said, "A private matter God the Exalted has covered, a burden God has sufficed, and a reward God the Exalted has sent forth." Then he descended and prayed two units of prayer, then said, "We have done what God the Exalted commanded." God the Exalted said: "And seek help through patience and prayer." And from Ibn al-Mubarak, that a son of his died, and a Magian who knew him came to offer condolences, and said to him, "It behooves the intelligent person to do today what the ignorant person does after five days." Ibn al-Mubarak said, "Write this down from him." And some of the scholars said, "Indeed, God tests the servant with trial after trial until he walks upon the earth having no sin." And al-Fudayl said, "Indeed, God, Mighty and Majestic, attends to His believing servant with trials just as a man attends to his family with good." And Hatim al-Asamm said, "Indeed, God, Mighty and Majestic, will argue on the Day of Resurrection against creation with four individuals for four categories: against the rich with Solomon, against the poor with the Messiah, against the slaves with Joseph, and against the sick with Job, the prayers of God be upon them." And it is narrated that Zechariah, peace be upon him, when he fled from the disbelievers from the Children of Israel and hid in a tree, and they became aware of that, so a saw was brought and the tree was sawn until the saw reached Zechariah's head, and he let out a groan. So God the Exalted revealed to him, "O Zechariah, if a second groan ascends from you, I will erase you from the register of prophethood." So Zechariah, peace be upon him, bit his finger until he severed two parts of it. And Abu Mas'ud al-Balkhi said, "Whoever is struck by a calamity and tears his garment or strikes his chest, it is as if he has taken a spear intending to fight his Lord, Mighty and Majestic, with it." And Luqman, may God have mercy on him, said to his son, "O my son, gold is tested by fire, and the righteous servant is tested by trial. So when God loves a people, He tests them. Thus, whoever is content, for him is contentment, and whoever is displeased, for him is displeasure." And al-Ahnaf ibn Qays said, "One day I awoke complaining of a toothache, and I said to my uncle, 'I did not sleep last night from the pain of the tooth until I pulled it out three times.' He said, 'You have made much of your tooth in one night, while this eye of mine has been gone for thirty years, and no one knew of it.'" And God the Exalted revealed to 'Aziz, peace be upon him, "If a trial befalls you, do not complain to My creation, but complain to Me, just as I do not complain of you to My angels when your faults and disgrace ascend. We ask God, from His immense gentleness and generosity, for His beautiful covering in this world and the next."

(Clarifying the Virtue of Blessing over Trial)

Perhaps you say, "These reports indicate that trial is better in this world than blessings, so should we ask God for trial?" I say, there is no basis for that, due to what is narrated from the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, that he used to seek refuge in his supplication from the trial of this world and the trial of the Hereafter, and he used to say, he and the prophets, peace be upon them, "Our Lord, give us good in this world and good in the Hereafter," and they used to seek refuge from the gloating of enemies and other things. And 'Ali, may God honor his countenance, said, "O God, I ask You for patience." So he, peace and blessings be upon him, said, "You have asked for trial, so ask Him for well-being." And al-Siddiq, may God the Exalted be pleased with him, narrated from the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, that he said, "Ask God for well-being, for no one has been given anything better than well-being except certainty." And he indicated by certainty the well-being of the heart from the sickness of ignorance and doubt, for the well-being of the heart is higher than the well-being of the body. And al-Hasan, may God have mercy on him, said, "The good in which there is no evil is well-being with gratitude, for how many are those upon whom blessings are bestowed who are not grateful." And Mutarrif ibn 'Abdullah said, "That I be granted well-being and be grateful is more beloved to me than that I be tested and be patient." And he, peace and blessings be upon him, said in his supplication, "And Your well-being is more beloved to me." This is clearer than to need evidence and citation for it. This is because trial becomes a blessing by two considerations: one, in relation to what is more than it, whether in this world or in religion; and the other, in relation to what is hoped for of reward. Therefore, we should ask God for the completion of blessing in this world and the removal of what is beyond it of trial, and we should ask Him for reward in the Hereafter for gratitude for His blessing, for He is capable of giving for gratitude what He does not give for patience.

If you say, "Indeed, some of them have said, 'I wish I were a bridge over the Fire, so all of creation could cross over it and be saved, and I would be in the Fire.'" And Sumnun, may God the Exalted have mercy on him, said:
            And I have no portion in other than You,
            So however You wish, test me.
So this from these individuals is a request for trial. Know that it is related from Sumnun the Lover, may God have mercy on him, that he was tested after this verse with the ailment of retention, and after that he would go around to the doors of the schools and say to the children, "Pray for your lying uncle." As for a person's love to be himself in the Fire to the exclusion of the rest of creation, it is not possible, but love can overcome the heart until the lover imagines of himself a love for such a thing. So from...

Drinking the cup of love brings intoxication, and the intoxicated speaks expansively. Were his intoxication to leave him, he would know that what overcame him was a state without true substance. Thus, what you hear from this genre is from the speech of lovers whose love has become excessive. The speech of lovers is delightful to hear, but one cannot rely upon it. As it is related that Fākhitah was being entreated by her husband, and she refused him. He said, "What prevents you from me? If I wished to turn the two worlds, along with the dominion of Solomon, upside down for your sake, I would do it." Solomon, peace be upon him, heard this and summoned him, reproaching him. He said, "O Prophet of God, the speech of lovers is not to be recounted." And it is as he said. The poet said:
            I desire union, and he desires abandonment,
            So I leave what I desire for what he desires.
This is also impossible. Its meaning is that I desire what he does not desire, for whoever desires union does not desire abandonment. So how could he desire abandonment, which he did not desire? Indeed, this speech cannot be deemed truthful except through two interpretations. The first is that this occurs in certain states, so that through it he may gain his beloved's pleasure, by which he attains union in the future. Thus, abandonment becomes a means to pleasure, and pleasure a means to the union of the beloved. The means to the beloved is beloved. Its example is like the lover of wealth who invests one dirham to gain two; for the love of the two dirhams, he forsakes the one dirham in the present. The second is that his beloved's pleasure becomes sought by him solely because it is his beloved's pleasure. He finds a delight in sensing his beloved's pleasure with him that surpasses his delight in seeing him, despite his aversion. At that point, it is conceivable that he would desire what contains that pleasure. Therefore, the state of some lovers has reached the point where their delight in tribulation, while sensing God's pleasure with them, is greater than their delight in well-being without sensing that pleasure. Thus, if they perceive His pleasure in tribulation, tribulation becomes more beloved to them than well-being. This is a state whose occurrence in the overpowering surges of love is not far-fetched, but it is not stable. Even if it were established as an example, is it a sound state, or a state necessitated by another state that came upon the heart, inclining it away from equilibrium? This requires examination, and detailing its verification is not suited to our present discussion. What has preceded makes clear that well-being is better than tribulation. So we ask God Almighty—who with His grace restrains from all His creation—for pardon and well-being in religion, this world, and the Hereafter, for us and for all Muslims.

(Clarification of Which is Superior: Patience or Gratitude)
Know that people have differed regarding this. Some have said patience is superior to gratitude; others said gratitude is superior; others said they are equal; and others said it differs according to circumstances. Each faction argued with discourse marked by severe confusion, far from sound acquisition. Thus, there is no point in prolonging by transmission; rather, hastening to manifest the truth is preferable. We say in clarifying this there are two stations. The first station is clarification in a lenient manner, which is to look at the apparent matter and not seek to investigate its reality. It is the clarification with which the common folk should be addressed, due to the deficiency of their understandings in grasping obscure realities. This genre of discourse is what preachers should rely upon, for the aim of their speech in addressing the common folk is their reform. The compassionate wet-nurse should not nourish the infant child with fattened birds and kinds of sweets, but with gentle milk. She must postpone fine foods for him until he becomes capable of bearing them through his strength and leaves behind the weakness inherent in his constitution. We say: This station in clarification forbids investigation and detailed analysis. Its requirement is to look at the apparent, understood from the sources of the Sacred Law. That necessitates favoring patience. For although many reports have come regarding the virtue of gratitude, when what has come regarding the virtue of patience is added to it, the virtues of patience become more numerous. Indeed, there are explicit texts regarding superiority, such as His saying, peace be upon him: "Among the best things you have been given are certainty and the resolve of patience." And in the report: "The most grateful person on earth will be brought, and God will reward him the reward of the grateful. Then the most patient person on earth will be brought and it will be said to him: 'Are you not content that We reward you as We rewarded this grateful one?' He will say, 'Yes, O Lord.' God Almighty will say, 'No. I bestowed favor upon him and he was grateful, and I tested you and you were patient. I shall multiply your reward for it.' So he is given multiples of the reward of the grateful one." And God Almighty has said: "Indeed, the patient will be given their reward without account." As for his saying, peace be upon him: "The grateful one who eats is in the rank of the patient one who fasts," this is evidence for the superiority in patience, for he mentioned that in a context of hyperbole to raise the rank of gratitude, thus equating it with patience. So this became the ultimate degree for it. Were it not understood from the Sacred Law that the rank of patience is elevated, equating gratitude with it would not be hyperbole for gratitude. It is like his saying, peace be upon him: "Friday is the Hajj of the poor, and the woman's striving is good conduct toward her husband," and like his saying, peace be upon him: "The drinker of wine is like an idolater..."

The idol[117]—and indeed, the thing being compared should always be of a higher rank. Likewise, his saying, peace be upon him: "Patience is half of faith" does not indicate that gratitude is its equal, just as his saying, peace be upon him: "Fasting is half of patience." For anything divisible into two parts, one is called a "half" even if there is disparity between them, just as it is said faith is knowledge and action, so action is half of faith. This does not indicate that action equals knowledge. In a report from the Prophet, peace be upon him: "The last of the prophets to enter Paradise is Solomon, son of David, peace be upon them, due to his kingdom; and the last of my companions to enter Paradise is 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, due to his wealth."[118] In another report: "Solomon will enter after the prophets by forty autumns."[119] And in the report: "All the gates of Paradise have two leaves except the gate of patience, for it has a single leaf, and the first to enter it are the people of affliction, with Job, peace be upon him, at their forefront."[120] All that has been narrated concerning the virtues of poverty indicates the virtue of patience, for patience is the state of the poor and gratitude the state of the wealthy. This is the station that satisfies and suffices the common people in appropriate admonition and instruction for the rectification of their religion.

The second station is the exposition we intend to elucidate for the people of knowledge and insight into the realities of matters through the path of unveiling and clarification. We say therein: Any two ambiguous matters cannot be balanced while ambiguous unless the reality of each is unveiled. Every unveiled matter comprises divisions; balancing the whole with the whole is not possible, rather the individual parts must be singled out for comparison until preponderance becomes clear. Patience and gratitude have many divisions and branches, so their ruling regarding preponderance and deficiency cannot be clarified in a general manner. We say: We have mentioned that these stations are composed of three matters: knowledge, states, and works. Gratitude, patience, and all other stations are likewise. When some of these three are weighed against others, it appears to observers of outward appearances that knowledge is sought for the sake of states, states are sought for the sake of works, and works are superior. As for the possessors of insight, the matter is the opposite for them: works are sought for the sake of states, states are sought for the sake of knowledge, so knowledge is superior, then states, then works. For whatever is sought for the sake of another, that other is inevitably superior to it. As for the individual parts of these three: works may be equal or may differ when compared to one another; likewise the individual states when compared; likewise the individual cognitions. The best cognitions are the sciences of unveiling, which are loftier than the sciences of transaction. Indeed, the sciences of transaction are inferior to transaction itself, for they are sought for the sake of transaction; their benefit is the rectification of work. The scholar of transaction is superior to the worshiper only if his knowledge is of general benefit, thus being superior relative to a specific action; otherwise, knowledge limited to action is not superior to action that is limited. We say: The benefit of rectifying action is the rectification of the heart's state. The benefit of rectifying the heart's state is that the majesty of God, Most High, in His Essence, Attributes, and Acts is unveiled to it. Thus, the loftiest of the sciences of unveiling is the knowledge of God, Glorified is He, which is the ultimate goal sought for its own sake, for through it happiness is attained—indeed, it is happiness itself. However, the heart may not perceive in this world that it is happiness itself; it only perceives that in the Hereafter. It is the free knowledge upon which there is no constraint, not bound by anything else. All other cognitions are, in relation to it, slaves and servants, for they are only sought for its sake. Since they are sought for its sake, their ranking is according to their usefulness in leading to the knowledge of God, Most High. For some cognitions lead to others, either directly or through many intermediaries. Thus, the fewer the intermediaries between it and the knowledge of God, Most High, the better it is. As for states, we mean by them the states of the heart in its purification and cleansing from the impurities of the world and the distractions of creation, so that when it is purified and clarified, the reality of the Truth becomes clear to it. Therefore, the virtues of states are according to their effect in rectifying, purifying, and preparing the heart to attain the sciences of unveiling. Just as polishing a mirror requires preceding states for the mirror, some closer to polish than others, so too are the states of the heart. The state closer or most conducive to the clarity of the heart is inevitably better than what is below it due to proximity to the objective. Similarly is the ranking of works, for their effect is in affirming the clarity of the heart and attracting states to it. Every work either attracts to it a state that prevents unveiling, causes darkness of the heart, and draws towards worldly adornments, or attracts to it a state that prepares for unveiling, causes clarity of the heart, and severs its attachments to the world. The name of the first is disobedience.

The second category is obedience and sins, which vary in their effect on the darkness and hardness of the heart. Likewise, acts of obedience vary in illuminating and purifying the heart, their degrees being according to the degrees of their effect. This differs according to differing circumstances. For we might say in absolute terms that supererogatory prayer is better than every other supererogatory act of worship, that Hajj is better than charity, and that standing in prayer at night is better than other acts. However, the truth of the matter is that for a rich man who possesses wealth but is overcome by miserliness and love of money to the point of withholding it, spending dirhams is better for him than standing in prayer for nights and fasting for days. For fasting suits one whose appetite has overcome him and he wishes to break it, or one whose satiety prevents the clarity of thought needed for the sciences of spiritual unveiling, so he wishes to purify his heart through hunger. As for this heedless one, if his state is not this state, he is not harmed by his appetite, nor is he occupied with a type of thought from which satiety prevents him. His occupation with fasting is a departure from his own state to the state of another. He is like a sick man complaining of a stomach ache; if he uses a headache remedy, he will not benefit from it. Rather, his duty is to look to the destructive trait that has mastered him. Miserliness, when obeyed, is among the destructive traits. Fasting for a hundred years and standing in prayer for a thousand nights will not remove from it an atom's weight. Rather, nothing removes it except spending wealth. So he must give in charity what he has. The details of this are from what we mentioned in the "Quarter of the Destructive Traits," so let him refer to it. Therefore, considering these circumstances, it differs. At that point, the insightful one knows that an absolute answer therein is erroneous. For if someone asked us, "Is bread better or water?" there would be no true answer except that bread is better for the hungry and water is better for the thirsty. If both are present, then look to the predominant need. If thirst is predominant, then water is better. If hunger is predominant, then bread is better. If they are equal, then they are equal. Likewise, if it is said, "Is oxymel better or lotus-flower drink?" it is not correct to answer it absolutely at all. Yes, if we were asked, "Is oxymel better or the absence of yellow bile?" we would say: the absence of yellow bile, for oxymel is intended for it, and what is intended for something else is inevitably better than it. Therefore, in spending wealth there is an action, which is expenditure, and a state is attained by it, which is the removal of miserliness and the expulsion of love of this world from the heart. The heart becomes prepared, due to the expulsion of love of this world from it, for the knowledge of God, the Exalted, and love of Him. So the best is knowledge, below it is the state, and below that is the action.

If you say: "The Sacred Law has urged actions and emphasized mentioning their merit, even seeking charities with His saying, 'Who is it that will lend Allah a goodly loan?' and He, the Exalted, said, 'And He accepts charities.' So how can the action and expenditure not be the best?" Know that when a physician praises a medicine, it does not indicate that the medicine is desired for its own sake or that it is better than the health and cure attained by it. Rather, actions are a treatment for the sicknesses of hearts. The sicknesses of hearts are something one is mostly unaware of. It is like leprosy on the face of one who has no mirror; he is not aware of it, and if it were mentioned to him, he would not believe it. The way with him is to exaggerate in praising washing the face with rosewater, for example, if rosewater removes the leprosy, so that he is urged by the excessive praise to persist in it, and thus his sickness is removed. For if it were mentioned to him that the objective is the removal of leprosy from your face, he might abandon the treatment and claim his face has no blemish. Let us give an example closer than this: We say: A man has a son whom he taught knowledge and the Quran, and he wanted to fix that in his memory so it would not leave him. He knew that if he ordered him to repeat and study so it would remain memorized for him, the son would say, "It is memorized, and I have no need for repetition and study," because he thinks what he memorizes now remains so forever. The man had slaves, so he ordered the son to teach the slaves and promised him something good for that, so that his motive would be abundant repetition through teaching. Perhaps the poor boy thinks the objective is teaching the slaves the Quran and that he has been employed to teach them. The matter becomes confusing for him, so he says, "Why have I been employed for the sake of the slaves? I am nobler than them and dearer to my father. I know that if my father wanted to teach the slaves, he could do so without imposing it on me. I know my father suffers no loss by the loss of these slaves, let alone their lack of knowledge of the Quran." So perhaps this poor one becomes lazy and abandons teaching them, relying on his father's self-sufficiency and his generosity in pardoning him. Thus he forgets the knowledge and the Quran and remains heedless, deprived without knowing it. A group has been deceived by such an illusion and taken the path of permissiveness, saying, "God, the Exalted, is independent of our worship and of borrowing from us. So what is the meaning of His saying, 'Who is it that will lend Allah a goodly loan?' If God willed to feed the poor..."

We would feed them, so we have no need to spend our wealth on them, as God Almighty said, narrating from the disbelievers: "And when it is said to them, 'Spend from what God has provided you,' the disbelievers say to the believers, 'Should we feed one whom, if God had willed, He would have fed?'" And they also said, "Had God willed, we would not have associated partners with Him, nor would our fathers." So observe how they were truthful in their speech and how they were destroyed by their truthfulness. Glory be to Him who, when He wills, destroys with truth, and when He wills, grants felicity through ignorance; He leads many astray by it and guides many by it. These people, when they assumed they were employed for the sake of the poor and needy—far exalted is God—and then said, "We have no share in the poor, and God has no share in us or our wealth, whether we spend or withhold," they were destroyed, just as the child was destroyed when he thought the parent's aim in employing him was for the sake of the slaves, unaware that the intended purpose was to establish the attribute of knowledge within his soul and affirm it in his heart, so that it would become the cause of his happiness in this world. This was merely the parent's gentleness in drawing him toward what contained his felicity. This example clarifies for you the misguidance of those who strayed from this path. Therefore, this poor person who takes your wealth extracts, through the medium of the wealth, the impurity of miserliness and love of this world from your inner self, for it is destructive to you. He is like a cupper who draws blood from you so that, with the blood's exit, the destructive ailment is removed from your inner self. Thus, the cupper serves you; you do not serve the cupper. Nor does the cupper cease being a servant by having an interest in doing something with the blood. And since charities are purifiers of the inner selves and cleanse them from the impurities of traits, the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, refrained from taking them and abstained from them, just as he forbade the cupper's earnings and called them the filth of people's wealth, and honored the people of his household by preserving them from it. The intended meaning is that actions have effects upon the heart, as previously mentioned in the Quarter of Destructive Traits, and the heart, according to the influence of these actions, becomes prepared to accept guidance and the light of knowledge. This is the universal statement and the fundamental principle to which one should return in knowing the virtues of actions, states, and knowledges. Let us now return to the specific matter we are in concerning patience and gratitude. We say regarding each of them: knowledge, state, and action. It is not permissible to contrast the knowledge of one with the state or action of the other; rather, each should be contrasted with its counterpart so that the correspondence becomes apparent, and after the correspondence, the superiority appears. Whenever the knowledge of the grateful is contrasted with the knowledge of the patient, they may revert to a single knowledge, for the knowledge of the grateful is to see, for example, the blessing of the eyes as from God Almighty, and the knowledge of the patient is to see blindness as from God. These are two concomitant, equal knowledges—if we consider affliction and calamities. We have already clarified that patience may be in obedience and against sin, and in these two, patience and gratitude unite, because patience in obedience is precisely gratitude for obedience, for gratitude reverts to directing God's blessing to what is intended by it through wisdom, and patience reverts to the steadfastness of the motive of religion in opposition to the motive of desire. Thus, patience and gratitude are two names for a single reality from two different perspectives. The steadfastness of the motive of religion in resisting the motive of desire is called patience in relation to the motive of desire, and called gratitude in relation to the motive of religion, for the motive of religion was created for this wisdom: to overthrow the motive of passion, and one has directed it to the intended purpose of wisdom. They are two expressions for a single meaning, so how can a thing be superior to itself? Therefore, the domains of patience are three: obedience, sin, and affliction. Their ruling in obedience and sin has become clear. As for affliction, it is an expression for the loss of a blessing. The blessing is either essential, like the eyes for example, or occurs in a place of need, like wealth exceeding sufficiency. Regarding the eyes, the patience of the blind person concerning them is by not showing complaint and showing contentment with God's decree, and not using blindness as a license for some sins. The gratitude of the sighted person for them, in terms of action, is through two matters: first, not using them to aid in sin; second, using them in obedience. Each of these two matters is not devoid of patience, for the blind person is spared patience regarding beautiful images because he does not see them, while the sighted person, if his sight falls upon beauty and he exercises patience, is grateful for the blessing of the eyes. But if he follows his gaze, he is ungrateful for the blessing of the eyes. Thus, patience has entered into his gratitude. Likewise, if he uses his eyes to aid in obedience, he also necessarily exercises patience in obedience. Then one may give thanks by contemplating the wonders of God's creation to attain thereby knowledge of God, Glorified and Exalted, and this gratitude becomes superior to patience. Were it not for this, the rank of Shu'ayb, peace be upon him—who was blind among the prophets—would be above the rank of Moses, peace be upon him.

Peace be upon him and the other prophets, for he endured the loss of sight, whereas Moses, peace be upon him, did not show such endurance, for example. Perfection would lie in a person being deprived of all limbs, leaving him like meat on a chopping block, but that is utterly impossible, for each of these organs is an instrument in religion; with its loss, that pillar of religion is lost, and gratitude for them lies in using them. They are instruments in religion, and that can only be through patience. As for what occurs in the realm of need, such as wealth beyond sufficiency, if one is given only what is necessary and is in need of what lies beyond it, then patience regarding it is a struggle—the struggle of poverty. The presence of excess is a blessing, and gratitude for it is to direct it to good deeds or to refrain from using it in disobedience. If patience is coupled with gratitude, which is directing it to obedience, then gratitude is superior because it encompasses patience as well, and it includes joy in the blessing of God Almighty, along with enduring the pain of directing it to the poor and refraining from using it for permissible indulgence. The outcome reverts to the principle that two things are better than one, and the whole is of a higher rank than a part. Yet there is a flaw here, for a comparison between the whole and its parts is not valid. However, if gratitude means not using it to aid in disobedience but rather directing it to permissible enjoyment, then patience here is superior to gratitude, and the patient poor person is better than the rich person who withholds his wealth, spending it on permissible things—not better than the rich person who directs his wealth to good deeds—because the poor person has struggled against his soul, broken its greed, and excelled in contentment with the decree of God Almighty. This state inevitably requires strength, whereas the rich person has followed his greed and obeyed his desires, though he limited himself to the permissible. The permissible provides leeway away from the forbidden, but strength is also necessary to abstain from the forbidden. However, the strength from which the patience of the poor arises is higher and more complete than the strength from which limiting indulgence to the permissible arises. The nobility lies in that strength which the action indicates, for actions are sought only for the states of the hearts, and that strength is a state of the heart that varies according to the strength of certainty and faith. Thus, whatever indicates increased strength in faith is undoubtedly superior. All that has been conveyed regarding the superiority of the reward of patience over the reward of gratitude in verses and traditions refers specifically to this rank, because what commonly comes to people’s minds regarding blessings and wealth is being enriched by them, and what commonly comes to minds regarding gratitude is for a person to say “Praise be to God” and not use the blessing to aid in disobedience—not to direct it to obedience. Therefore, patience is better than gratitude—meaning the patience understood by the common people is better than the gratitude understood by the common people. It is to this meaning specifically that Junayd, may God have mercy on him, alluded when he was asked about patience and gratitude, which is superior. He said: “Praise is not for the rich due to possession, nor for the poor due to lack. Rather, praise for both lies in their fulfillment of the conditions incumbent upon them. The condition for the rich accompanies him in what is upon him—things that suit his nature, give him enjoyment and pleasure. The poor person accompanies him in what is upon him—things that suit his nature, cause him restraint and distress. If both are fulfilling for God Almighty the condition incumbent upon them, then the one whose nature is afflicted and distressed is in a more complete state than the one whose nature is indulged and gratified.” The matter is as he said, and it is correct within the categories of patience and gratitude in the last category we mentioned, and he intended nothing else. It is said that Abu al-‘Abbas ibn ‘Ata’ disagreed with him on this, saying the grateful rich person is better than the patient poor person. Junayd prayed against him, and he was afflicted with what befell him of trials—the killing of his children, loss of his wealth, and loss of his mind for fourteen years. He would say, “Junayd’s prayer struck me,” and he reverted to preferring the patient poor person over the grateful rich person. Whenever you consider the meanings we have mentioned, you will know that each of the two views has a valid aspect in some circumstances. Sometimes a patient poor person is better than a grateful rich person, as previously stated, and sometimes a grateful rich person is better than a patient poor person. That is the rich person who sees himself as like the poor, for he does not retain for himself from wealth except what is necessary, and the remainder he directs to good deeds or holds onto with the belief that he is a custodian for the needy and the poor, merely waiting for a need to arise so he may direct it thereto. Then, when he spends, he does not do so seeking status or reputation, nor out of mere habit, but rather as fulfilling the duty to God Almighty in caring for His servants. This is better than the patient poor person.

If you say: “This does not weigh heavily on the soul, whereas poverty weighs heavily on the poor person, because this one feels the pleasure of ability, and that one feels the pain of patience. If he is pained by parting with wealth, that is compensated by the pleasure in the ability to spend.” Know that what you see is that whoever spends his wealth willingly and with a content heart...

A more complete state is that of one who spends while being miserly with it, for he wrests it from himself by force. We have detailed this previously in the Book of Repentance. Thus, causing the self pain is not sought for its own sake, but to discipline it. This is akin to striking a hunting dog. The disciplined dog is more complete than the dog needing to be struck, even if it is patient under the strike. Therefore, one needs pain and struggle at the beginning and does not need them at the end. Rather, the end is that what was painful to him becomes delightful to him, just as learning becomes delightful to the intelligent boy after having been painful to him at first. Since all people, save the very few, are at the beginning—indeed, far before the beginning—like children, Junayd made the general statement that the one whose attribute is painful is superior. This is correct, as he intended, regarding the generality of creation. Therefore, if you do not specify the answer and make it general, intending the majority, then state generally that patience is superior to gratitude, for it is correct according to the meaning previously conveyed to understandings. If you seek precision, then specify, for patience has degrees. The lowest of them is abandoning complaint while disliking [the affliction]. Beyond it is contentment, which is a station beyond patience. Beyond it is gratitude for the affliction, which is beyond contentment, for patience is with pain, and contentment is possible with that which contains no pain and no joy, while gratitude is only possible for something beloved that brings joy. Likewise, gratitude has many degrees; we have mentioned the utmost of them. Included in its totality are matters lesser than it: the servant's shame at the succession of God's blessings upon him is gratitude; his recognition of his shortcoming in gratitude is gratitude; excusing himself for the lack of gratitude is gratitude; knowledge of God's immense forbearance and the shelter of His covering is gratitude; acknowledgment that blessings originate from God, the Exalted, without any deserving is gratitude; knowing that gratitude itself is also a blessing from God's blessings and a bestowal from Him is gratitude; good humility toward blessings and lowliness within them is gratitude; and gratitude to the intermediaries is gratitude, for the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: "Whoever does not thank people has not thanked God." We have explained the reality of this in the Book of the Secrets of Zakat. Lack of objection and good manners before the Benefactor is gratitude; receiving blessings with good acceptance and magnifying what is small among them is gratitude. The individual instances of works and states subsumed under the name of gratitude and patience are innumerable, and they are different degrees. So how can a general statement preferring one over the other be possible, except by way of intending a specific meaning with a general expression, as has come in the reports and traditions? It has been narrated from one of them that he said: I saw in some travels a very old man, advanced in years. I asked him about his state, and he said: At the beginning of my life, I loved my cousin, and she likewise loved me. It happened that she was married to me. On the night of her bridal procession, I said, 'Let us rise to spend this night in gratitude to God, the Exalted, for uniting us.' So we prayed that night, and neither of us turned to the other. When the second night came, we said the same, and we prayed the entire night. For seventy or eighty years now, we have been in that state every night. Is that not so, O so-and-so? The old woman said: It is as the Shaykh says. Consider these two: if they had been patient under the affliction of separation, or if God had not brought them together, and then compare the patience of separation to the gratitude of union in this manner, it will not be hidden from you that this gratitude is superior. Therefore, there is no stopping at the realities of what is superior except by specification, as preceded. And God knows best.

[7] The hadith "The Hajj is 'Arafah" has been previously mentioned in the chapter on Hajj.

[8] The hadith "The best of deeds are those which the souls are averse to" has no established chain of narration as a Marfu' (attributed to the Prophet). Rather, it is a statement of 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, as reported by Ibn Abi al-Dunya in his book "Muhasabat al-Nafs" (The Reckoning of the Soul).

[9] The hadith of 'Ata' from Ibn 'Abbas: "He entered upon the Ansar and said, 'Are you believers?' They remained silent. 'Umar said, 'Yes, O Messenger of God.'" Al-Tabarani reported it in "Al-Awsat" through the narration of Yusuf ibn Maymun, and it is considered a rejected (Munkar) narration from 'Ata'.

[10] The hadith "Patience in the face of what is disliked holds abundant good" was reported by Al-Tirmidhi from a hadith of Ibn 'Abbas, and it has been previously mentioned.

[11] The hadith "If patience were a man, he would be noble" was reported by Al-Tabarani from a hadith of 'A'ishah. In its chain is Subayh ibn Dinar, whom Al-'Uqayli deemed weak.

[12] The hadith "Whoever dies, his Resurrection has begun" was reported by Ibn Abi al-Dunya in his book "Al-Mawt" (Death) from a hadith of Anas with a weak chain.

[13] The hadith "Sufficient is death as an admonisher" was reported by Al-Bayhaqi in "Al-Shu'ab" from a hadith of 'A'ishah. In its chain is Al-Rabi' ibn Badr, who is weak. Al-Tabarani also reported it from a hadith of 'Uqbah ibn 'Amir. It is well-known as a saying of Al-Fudayl ibn 'Iyad, reported by Al-Bayhaqi in "Al-Zuhd" (Asceticism).

[14] The hadith "O Allah, make easy for Muhammad the agonies of death" was reported by Al-Tirmidhi, who said it is Gharib (uncommon). Al-Nasa'i reported it in "Al-Yawm wa al-Laylah" and Ibn Majah from a hadith of 'A'ishah with the wording: "O Allah, help me with the agonies of death."

[15] The hadith "I and the guardian of an orphan will be like these two (in Paradise)" was reported by Al-Bukhari from a hadith of Sahl ibn Sa'd, and it has been previously mentioned.

[16] The hadith "The Hajj is 'Arafah" is reported by the compilers of the Sunan from a hadith of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Ya'mur, and it has been previously mentioned in the chapter on Hajj.

[17] The hadith "The intelligent one is he who holds himself to account..." has been previously mentioned in the chapter on dispraising self-delusion.

[18] The hadith "Worship Allah with contentment, but if you cannot, then patience in the face of what you dislike holds abundant good" is reported by Al-Tirmidhi from a hadith of Ibn 'Abbas, and it has been previously mentioned.

[19] The hadith "Children are a cause of cowardice, miserliness, and grief" is reported by Abu Ya'la al-Mawsili from a hadith of Abu Sa'id, and it has been previously mentioned.

[20] The hadith "When he saw his son Al-Hasan stumbling in his shirt, he descended from the pulpit..." is reported by the compilers of the Sunan from a hadith of Buraydah. They said (it mentions) Al-Hasan and Al-Husayn. Al-Tirmidhi said it is Hasan Gharib.

[21] The hadith "Actions are but by intentions" is agreed upon (Muttafaq 'alayh) from a hadith of 'Amr, and it has been previously mentioned.

[22] The hadith "The emigrant (Muhajir) is he who abandons evil, and the striver (Mujahid) is he who strives against his own desires." Ibn Majah reported the first part and Al-Nasa'i in "Al-Kubra" reported the second part. Both are from a hadith of Fadalah ibn 'Ubayd with two sound chains, and they have been previously mentioned.

[23] The hadith "Indeed, backbiting is more severe than fornication" has been previously mentioned in the chapter on the afflictions of the tongue.

[24] The hadith about dividing wealth once and the statement of a Bedouin man, "This is a division not intended for the sake of Allah," is agreed upon (Muttafaq 'alayh) from a hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, and it has been previously mentioned.

[25] The hadith "Maintain ties with him who severs ties with you..." has been previously mentioned.

[26] The hadith "I ask You for a certainty which makes the calamities of this world easy for me" is reported by Al-Tirmidhi, Al-Nasa'i, and Al-Hakim who authenticated it, from a hadith of Ibn 'Umar. Al-Tirmidhi graded it as Hasan. It has been previously mentioned in the chapter on supplications.

[27] The hadith: "Allah said, 'When I direct a calamity towards a servant of Mine concerning his body, his child, or his wealth, and he meets it with beautiful patience...'" is reported by Ibn 'Adi from a hadith of Anas with a weak chain.

[28] The hadith "Awaiting relief with patience is an act of worship" is reported by Al-Quda'i in "Musnad al-Shihab" from a hadith of Ibn 'Umar and Ibn 'Abbas. Ibn Abi al-Dunya reported it in "Al-Faraj ba'd al-Shiddah" from a hadith of 'Ali without the phrase "with patience." Abu Sa'id al-Malini also reported it in "Musnad al-Sufiyyah" from a hadith of Ibn 'Umar. All these chains are weak. Al-Tirmidhi reported from a hadith of Ibn Mas'ud: "The best of worship is awaiting relief," and it has been previously mentioned in the chapter on supplications.

[29] The hadith "No believing servant is afflicted by a calamity and says as Allah the Exalted has commanded, 'Indeed we belong to Allah, and indeed to Him we return,'..." is reported by Muslim from a hadith of Umm Salamah.

[30] The hadith of Anas: "He said, 'O Jibril, what is the reward for one whose two precious ones are taken?'..." is reported by Al-Tabarani in "Al-Awsat" through the narration of Abu Zalal al-Qasimili—his name is Hilal, one of the weak narrators—from Anas. And it was reported...

Bukhari reports with the wording that Allah, the Almighty, said: "When I test My servant with his two beloved ones and he remains patient, I compensate him with Paradise for them." Ibn 'Abd al-Barr and Abu Ya'la report with the wording: "When I take the two precious ones of My servant, I am not content with any recompense for him less than Paradise." I said: "O Messenger of Allah, even if it is one?" He said: "Even if it is one." In its chain is Sa'id bin Sulaym. Ibn 'Adi said: "It is weak."

[31] A Hadith in which Allah says: "When I afflict My servant with a trial and he remains patient and does not complain of Me to his visitors, I replace his flesh with flesh better than his." The Hadith is in Malik's Muwatta' from the narration of 'Ata' bin Yasar from Abu Sa'id. End. And 'Abbad bin Kathir is weak. Al-Bayhaqi reported it as Mawquf (stopped) on Abu Hurayrah.

[32] A Hadith: "From honoring Allah and knowing His right is that you do not complain of your pain nor mention your calamity." I did not find it Marfu' (attributed to the Prophet). Rather, Ibn Abi al-Dunya reported it in "Al-Marad wa al-Kaffarat" from the narration of Sufyan from some jurists who said: "From patience is that you do not speak of your calamity, nor of your pain, nor praise yourself."

[33] The Hadith of al-Rumaysa', Umm Sulaym: "My son died, and my husband Abu Talhah was absent. So I arose and covered him in a corner of the house." The Hadith is in al-Tabarani and from his route Abu Nu'aym in "Al-Hilyah." The story is in the two Sahihs from the narration of Anas with some differences.

[34] The Hadith: "Indeed Allah despises the idle youth." I did not find it.

[35] The Hadith: "A glance is a poisoned arrow from the arrows of Iblis." It has preceded more than once.

[36] The Hadith: "You should marry, and whoever cannot, then upon him is fasting." The Hadith has preceded in the chapter on Marriage.

[37] The Hadith: "Indeed this religion is firm, so proceed into it with gentleness." Ahmad from the narration of Anas and al-Bayhaqi from the narration of Jabir. It has preceded in the chapter on Devotions.

[38] The Hadith: "Do not be harsh with this religion, for whoever is harsh with it, it will overcome him." It has preceded regarding it.

[39] The Hadith: "The grateful eater is in the position of the patient faster." Al-Bukhari alluded to it, and al-Tirmidhi reported it with a chain and graded it Hasan, as did Ibn Majah and Ibn Hibban from the narration of Abu Hurayrah. Ibn Majah also reported it from the narration of Sinan bin Sanah, and in its chain there is a difference.

[40] The Hadith of 'Ata': "I entered upon 'A'ishah and said to her: 'Inform us of the most amazing thing you saw from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him.' She said: 'All of his matters were amazing...'" The Hadith is about his weeping in the night prayer. Abu al-Shaykh and Ibn Hibban reported it in "The Character of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him," and from his route Ibn al-Jawzi in "Al-Wafa'." In it is Abu Janab, whose name is Yahya bin Abi Habbah; the majority weakened him. Ibn Hibban reported it in his Sahih from the narration of 'Abd al-Malik bin Abi Sulayman from 'Ata' without her saying: "All of his matters were amazing." And it is in Muslim from the narration of 'Urwah from 'A'ishah, limited to the end of the Hadith.

[41] The Hadith: "A call will be made on the Day of Resurrection: 'Let those who praise much rise.'" Al-Tabarani, Abu Nu'aym in "Al-Hilyah," and al-Bayhaqi in "Al-Shu'ab" reported it from the narration of Ibn 'Abbas with the wording: "The first to be called to Paradise are those who praise much." In it is Qays bin al-Rabi'; the majority weakened him.

[42] The Hadith: "Praise is the cloak of the Most Merciful." I did not find a basis for it. In the Sahih from the narration of Abu Hurayrah is: "Grandeur is His cloak." It has preceded in the chapter on Knowledge.

[43] The Hadith of 'Umar: "Let one of you adopt a tongue that remembers and a heart that is grateful." The Hadith has preceded in the chapter on Marriage.

[44] The Hadith: "Whoever says 'Subhan Allah' (Glory be to Allah), for him are ten good deeds." It has preceded in the chapter on Supplications.

[45] The Hadith: "The best remembrance is 'La ilaha illa Allah' (There is no god but Allah), and the best container is 'Alhamdulillah' (Praise be to Allah)." Al-Tirmidhi reported it and graded it Hasan, as did al-Nasa'i in "Al-Yawm wa al-Laylah," Ibn Majah, and Ibn Hibban from the narration of Jabir.

[46] The Hadith: "There is nothing among the remembrances that is multiplied like 'Alhamdulillah' (Praise be to Allah) is multiplied." I did not find it Marfu'. Rather, Ibn Abi al-Dunya reported it in the book "Al-Shukr" from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: "It is said that praise is the speech most multiplied."

[47] The Hadith: "He, peace be upon him, said to a man: 'How have you become in the morning?' He said: 'In good condition.' He repeated the question until on the third time he said: 'In good condition, I praise Allah and thank Him.' He said: 'This is what I wanted from you.'" Al-Tabarani reported it in "Al-Du'a'" from the narration of al-Fudayl bin 'Amr, Marfu', similar to it. He said on the third: "I praise Allah." And this is Mu'dal (with a broken chain). He reported it in "Al-Mu'jam al-Kabir" from the narration of 'Abdullah bin 'Amr; it does not contain the repetition of the question, and he said: "I praise Allah to you." In it is Rashid bin Sa'd; the majority weakened him due to his poor memory. Malik reported it in the Muwatta'.
